Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Abstract
5-(Hydroxymethyl)furfural (HMF) and the imidazoles 2-acetyl-4(5)-tetrahydroxybutylimidazole
(THI), 2-methylimidazole (2-MI) and 4-methylimidazole (4-MI) constitute process contaminants
formed during the manufacture of caramel colours and are subsequently carried on to food
and beverages in which caramel colours are used.
Despite, that the toxicological proп¬Ғle of
HMF is still under debate, all four compounds are recognised as harmful substances that may
pose a risk to human health. Thus, Ultra-High-performance Liquid Chromatography-tandem
Mass Spectrometry (UHPLC-MS/MS) methods were developed, optimised and validated for
the quantiп¬Ғcation of HMF, and the simultaneous quantiп¬Ғcation of the imidazoles, in caramel
colours. Sample preparation included dilution and п¬Ғltration for HMF, and dilution, strong cation
exchange solid-phase extraction (SPE) and п¬Ғltration for the imidazoles. A limit of detection
(LOD) of 300, 0.055, 0.085 and 0.974 mg/kg; and recoveries of 80.0-110.7%, 98.4-107.5%, 84.2-
103.8% and 87.1-110.9%, were obtained for HMF, THI, 2-MI and 4-MI, respectively. In addition,
the Coeп¬ғcients of variation (CV%) for In-House reproducibility were all вүӨ3.6% for the imidazoles
and вүӨ12.0% for levels that could be validated for HMF. The newly developed methods were
applied to analyse received manufacturer samples (n=35) to investigate if certain production
conditions inп¬Ӯuenced the constituent levels. A reduced ammonia seemed to lower the content
of THI and 4-MI (also on an equivalent colour basis).
Finally, an exposure evaluation was
conducted for two Danish population groups (adults and adolescents) to assess if the exposure
to the contaminants via caramel colours pose a public health concern. The margin of exposure
(MOE) was found to be below the threshold for HMF and 4-MI, implying that these may
pose a health risk for the Danish population. More research within the area of caramel colour
production with the aim to lower the content of certain contaminants could be a necessity.

Preface
This master thesis represents the culmination of six memorable years of education at the Tech-
nical University of Denmark, which in so many ways have developed us not only academically
but also personally. The thesis was performed at the National Food Institute allowing us to
work with our shared passion for analytical chemistry and food safety.
Many people have been involved on a daily basis. In fact, we would not have been able to accom-
plish the work behind this report without the great help from the brilliant people working in the
Research Group for Analytical Food Chemistry at the National Food Institute. Among those,
the laboratory technicians Liljana Petrevska, Line Christoп¬Җersen, Maud Bering Andersen and
Yuka Omura Lund which together have provided us with safety tours in building 202, training
in use of the equipment, purchasing of materials and chemicals, and last but least have been a
great company. Furthermore, a warm thank to chemist Mikael Pedersen for the indispensable
help in relation to the HPLC-MS/MS including theoretical and practical knowledge sharing, and
trouble shooting. Having you as a partner for discussion has been a great pleasure. We would
also like to express our appreciation to our supervisors, Associate Professor Lene Duedahl-Olesen
and Postdoc Amelie Sina Wilde. A warm thank to Lene Duedahl-Olesen for giving us the great
opportunity to gain experience within the п¬Ғeld of analytical chemistry, and for giving us weekly
direction and constructive feedback in regards to the overall scope of the project as well as the
structure and content of this thesis. A thank to Amalie Sina Wilde for giving us both practical
and theoretical guidance. It is safe to say that without your great help with direction of the
practical work, smart inputs and п¬Ӯexible work hours, we would not have been able to overcome
the obstacles we have encountered during the 4.5 п¬Ғve months of laboratory work.
From outside the Research Group for Analytical Food Chemistry, we would like to thank pro-
fessor SГёren KegnГҰs and PhD student Mikkel Hansen, for each letting us borrow a vacuum oven
and instructing us in the use of it. Moreover, a thank goes to engineer Peter Reimer Stubbe
for providing us with a safety tour in building 222, and п¬Ғnally, our gratitude goes to Associate
Professor Anoop Kumar Sharma for giving us a brief but very useful brush up in chemical risk
assessment.
Finally, a huge thank to friends, family and colleagues for supporting us throughout this busy
period. Among those, a special thank to chemical engineer Jacob Seneca Nielsen for giving us
outstanding feedback on the report and for giving us the opportunity to perform a rehearsal at

Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
1
Introduction
Caramel colours are brown-black hygroscopic powders or viscous liquids that have been used as
colouring agents in several foods and beverages for more than 150 years [1]. They are authorised
as food additives in the European Union (EU) and are categorized by the Joint FAO/WHO
Expert Committee on Food Additives (JECFA) according to the type of reactants used in the
manufacturing process [2, 3, 4]. The following constitute the four distinct classes that caramel
colours are divided into: Class I - Plain Caramel (E150a); Class II - Sulphite Caramel (E150b);
Class III - Ammonia Caramel (E150c), and Class IV - Sulphite Ammonia Caramel (E150d) from
which class III and IV are the most widely used food coloring agents by volume [5].
All four classes vary in colour intensity and hue, and hold an odor of burnt sugar and a slightly
bitter but satisfactory п¬Ӯavor [1].
Since they are usually applied in signiп¬Ғcant low amounts,
the taste is rarely notable in п¬Ғnal products. Besides their colouring capability, they may also
contribute with other functionalities such as having a stabilising eп¬Җect on colloidal systems and
possess emulsifying properties. For this reason they are known to facilitate п¬Ӯavor retention and
dispersion of water-insoluble matter such as essential oils contributing to the п¬Ӯavor [6]. Caramel
colours are used in a wide range of products, where some of the typical applications are as
follows: Class I is often applied in bread croutons, cereals, nutrition bars, rice cakes and stuп¬Җ-
ings; Class II is usually included in distilled spirits (e.g. cognac and sherry); Class III in bread,
cake mixes, muп¬ғns and other baked goods, cereals, dark beers and frostings, and Class IV in
baked goods, cereals, confectionery, soft drinks and snack foods [1]. A more extensive list of
food categories in which caramel colours are used - including associated provisions are covered
in the Codex General Standard for Food Additives (GSFA) provided by the Codex Alimentarius
Commission [7].
Traditionally, caramel colours were known as caramels and were made by heating up sugars at
temperatures about 200в—ҰC until a loss of 10-15% of the initial weight was obtained. Frequently,
cane sugar was used but other sugars such as glucose syrup, molasses, honey or even mixtures
have also been applied [6]. Nowadays, caramel products utilized only for colouring purposes go
under the term вҖҷcaramel coloursвҖҷ in order to distinguish them from caramels used in the manu-
facturing of products like confectionery items to give them the characteristic п¬Ӯavour of caramel.
Today, caramel colours are manufactured by applying heat to food-grade carbohydrates under
strictly controlled process conditions. The carbohydrates can be heated to various temperatures
and at various pressures together with reactants such as acids, alkalis, ammonium compounds
and sulп¬Ғte compounds to facilitate the production of caramel colours with strong colour inten-
sities [1].
The п¬Ғrst commercially available caramel colours in the United States (US) were manufactured
back in 1863. At that time no regulations were established for the incorporation of caramel
colours in food products. Thus, several decades passed before the US Food and Drug Admin-
istration (FDA) in 1940 began to regulate them. Despite diп¬Җerent classes were widely known
Page 1 of 143
==PAGE:10==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
and used commercially, no distinction was required on label declarations. Instead, collective
terms such as вҖҷColoured with CaramelвҖҷ or вҖҷBurnt sugar ColouringвҖҷ applied to all classes ac-
cording to a ruling issued in 1940 [6]. During the 1940s, the use of caramel colours were on
several occasions declared harmless by FDA and after the establishment of the вҖҷFood Additives
Amendment of 1958вҖҷ, they were put on the list of Generally Recognized As Safe (GRAS) sub-
stances (21CFR В§182.1235 - Caramel) [1, 6]. Subsequent to the вҖҷColor Additive Amendments
of 1960вҖҷ, caramel colours were in 1963 permanently registered as colouring agents for use in
human foods exempt from FDA batch certiп¬Ғcation (21CFR В§73.85).
Today, FDA still does
not distinguish between the diп¬Җerent classes in terms of regulation and provides no limitations
on usage. Instead, amounts should be consistent with Good Manufacturing Practice (GMP)
[6, 8]. The food safety organization вҖҷFood Chemical CodexвҖҷ (FCC) similarly recognise the four
classes but has a caramel colour monograph in which a single speciп¬Ғcation is applicable to all [1].
Today, the majority of all countries specify caramel colours according to the distinct classes and
have designated food and beverage categories in which they can be utilised. In EU, the maximum
permitted use level of all caramel colour classes are set as вҖҷquantum satisвҖҷ meaning the amount
which is enough, according to the commission regulation (EU) No 1129/2011 of 11 November
2011 amending Annex II to Regulation (EC)No 1333/2008 [9]. Since 1965, where JECFA for the
п¬Ғrst time reviewed caramel colours, several activities have been executed to ensure safe usage
of caramel colours as food additives. Among those, toxicological studies, exposure evaluations,
and safety evaluations by JECFA and the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA) resulting in
the establishment of a group Acceptable Daily Intake (ADI) of 300 mg/kg bw/day for all classes
and an individual ADI of 100 mg/kg bw/day for class III [10]. In addition, attention have been
brought to certain heat-derived constituents that may pose a health risk. This includes, among
others; 5-(Hydroxymethyl)furfural (HMF), 2-acetyl-4(5)-tetrahydroxybutylimidazole (THI) and
the constitutional isomers 2-methylimidazole (2-MI) and 4-methylimidazole (4-MI). All four
compounds can be formed in the manufacture of caramel colour class III and IV via the Mail-
lard reaction when reducing sugars such as glucose react with ammonia [11]. Additionally, HMF
can be found in all caramel colour classes since the formation of this compound is not restricted
to the Maillard reaction but can also be formed via caramelization, in which nitrogenous com-
pounds are not required.
Table 1 summarises the classiп¬Ғcation of caramel colours and includes selected speciп¬Ғcations. In
this, it can be seen that maximum levels (ML) have been placed for THI restricted to caramel
colour class III, and 4-MI in class III and IV, both expressed on an equivalent colour basis.
To express a constituent level on an equivalent colour basis, one must quantify the compound,
determine the solids content and measure the colour intensity deп¬Ғned as вҖҷthe absorbance of a
0.1% (w/v) solution of Caramel Colour solids in water in a 1 cm cell at 610 nmвҖҷ from which
the content can be expressed in matter of a product having a colour intensity of 0.1 absorbance
units. [12]. Equivalent colour basis serves as a common ground for the regulation of caramel
colours being highly variable products, not only among the four classes but also within each
class.
No ML has been established for HMF nor 2-MI, but in a a scientiп¬Ғc opinion on the
Page 2 of 143
==PAGE:11==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
re-evaluation of caramel colours provided by EFSA, it is recognised that HMF may be present
in levels of concern. For this reason, a ML for HMF is taken into consideration. Furthermore,
the EFSA panel welcomes research work regarding process conditions in relation to the forma-
tion of heat-derived contaminants [10]. When new legislation is put forward; often, an urge
for new and improved methods arises. For the simultaneous analysis of THI, 2-MI and 4-MI,
the current literature reports challenges with regards to keeping a short analysis time while re-
maining a suп¬ғcient chromatographic separation between the imidazoles. Moreover, THI tends
to elute at the same time as non-retained matrix compounds when utilising a C18 column [4, 13].

2
Statement of Intent
HMF and the imidazoles THI, 2-MI and 4-MI are all unwanted hetero-cyclic compounds that
can be formed during the production of caramel colours. Analysis of HMF is possible by LC-UV.
However, inclusion of the imidazoles requires additional clean up and use of LC-MS/MS. This
project therefore includes development and validation of a quantitative analytical method to
be used for quantiп¬Ғcation of the aforementioned constituents in caramel colour. The developed
method should be applied to manufacturer samples to investigate if certain process conditions
have an inп¬Ӯuence on the content of these compounds. Finally, an exposure evaluation of the
included process contaminants is relevant.
Learning objectives:
вҖў Application of LC-MS/MS theory on analysis of caramel colour
вҖў Evaluate own method validation
вҖў Application of a newly developed method and evaluation of own results
вҖў Evaluate the human exposure to the compounds

Theory
3.1
Heat-Derived Contaminants in Caramel Colours
Maillard and caramelization reactions are two reactions that takes place when sugars and a
source of nitrogen are heated together. Caramelization reactions mainly takes place when tem-
peratures are above 120 в—ҰC, pH is between 3-9 and at low water activity. Maillard reactions is
favored when temperatures are above 50 в—ҰC and the pH is between 4-7 with an intermediate
water content [14, 15].
5-(Hydroxymethyl)furfural (HMF), 2-acetyl-4(5)-tetrahydroxybutylimidazole (THI) and the con-
stitutional isomers 2-methylimidazole (2-MI) and 4-methylimidazole (4-MI) are all heterocyclic
Maillard reaction products that may be formed during the manufacturing of caramel colours
depending on the type of reactants present [16]. In absence of nitrogen containing compounds
such as amino acids or ammonia, HMF can also be formed via caramelization [17]. The chemical
structure of each compound is depicted in п¬Ғgure 1 and chemical information can be seen in table
2.
Figure 1: The chemical structure of (a) THI, (b) 2-MI, (c) HMF and (d) 4-MI [16].

HMF that is a well-known byproduct of heat treatment constitute a substance that for a long
time has been used to monitor the degree of changes that occur as a result of thermal processing
[25]. Besides being an useful indicator, much attention has been drawn to its harmful potential
which will be described in further detail in section 3.1.1.
Through the Maillard reaction a
source of nitrogen, added ammonia in the case of caramel colours, and a reducing sugar react in
Amadori rearrangement, dehydration and fragmentation to form HMF. A reaction scheme for
the formation of HMF through the Maillard reaction can be seen in п¬Ғgure 2. Furthermore, a
reaction scheme for the formation of HMF through caramelization can be seen in п¬Ғgure 3. The
expected concentrations of HMF in each caramel colour can be seen in table 1.
Figure 2: Purposed formation of HMF via the Maillard reaction [26].
Page 6 of 143
==PAGE:15==

Spring 2021
Figure 3: Purposed formation of HMF via caramelization reactions [17].
THI, 2-MI and 4-MI, are only expected to be found in caramel colours of class III and IV because
their formation requires a source of nitrogen. In 2012 these were categorised as 2B agents by the
International Agency for Research on Cancer (IARC) [27]. This means that their toxicological
proп¬Ғles makes them possibly carcinogenic to humans which will be described in further detail
in section 3.1.1. As opposed to HMF, ML have been established for THI, 2-MI and 4-MI which
is listed in table 1.
Several formation pathways of 2-MI and 4-MI have been described in literature. The formation
takes place when either ammonia (caramel colour class III) or ammonium salts (caramel colour
class IV) react with reducing sugars such as glucose and fructose. Wu et al. [11] investigated
the formation of 2-MI and 4-MI and proposed two formation pathways and possible mechanisms
that could explain the much lower content of 2-MI compared to 4-MI. The lower content of 2-MI
compared to 4-MI arises from the fact that several reactions leads to the formation of 4-MI where
only one leads to 2-MI. Furthermore the reaction that leads to the formation of 2-MI have an
unfavorable reaction step due to steric hindrance in the transition state. No information could
be found on the formation of THI, but since it is an imidazole it is thought to follow similar
reaction pathways as 2-MI and 4-MI seen in 4.

3.1.1
Toxicology of HMF, THI, 2-MI and 4-MI
HMF
HMF have been studied on many occasions for potential genotoxicity and mutagenicity eп¬Җects.
It is generally noted that these studies were carried out under strongly varying conditions and
the studies were not primarily focused on HMF. The results of these studies are therefore not
conclusive [28, 26]. NTP conducted in 2010 a 2-year feed study in mice and rats together with
mutagenicity assays in S. Typhimurium and E. Coli. The mutagenicity assays showed slight
mutagenic tendencies of HMF in S. Typhimurium but 8 other mutagenicity assays showed no
mutagenic tendencies of HMF. The 2-year feed study conclude that HMF only showed dose
related increase of carcinogenic activity in the liver of female mice at the lowest dose tested
therefor it was not possible to establish a no-observed-adverse-eп¬Җect level (NOAEL). The data
indicate that HMF is indirect carcinogenic via a threshold mechanism (e.g. oxidative stress).
NTP also conducted a 90-day study which established a NOAEL of 80-100 mg/kg bw/day for
acute and sub-acute toxicity related to the kidneys in mice [29]. More data is needed to establish
a NOAEL for the potential carcinogenic eп¬Җects of HMF [10].
Page 8 of 143
==PAGE:17==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
THI
THI has been shown to be immunosuppressive. The immunosuppressive activity of THI is be-
lieved to stem from the inhibition of a certain enzyme called S1P lyase. S1P is a lysophospholipid
which plays an important role in regulation of the migration of immune cells from the lymphoid
tissue into the blood stream. The intake of THI result in higher levels of S1P in the lymphoid
tissue which prevents immune cells to migrate to the blood stream [30, 31, 32]. Though a 90-day
oral toxicity study in rats a NOAEL of 380 Вөg/kg bw/day for male rats and 120 Вөg/kg bw/day
for female rats was established for the immunosuppressant eп¬Җects of THI. As noted by Houben
and Penninks [33] the 90-day studies in rats were done with a low level of B6 in the feed which is
comparable to that of deп¬Ғciencies in humans and for that reason humans might be less sensitive
to caramel colour and THI.
2-MI
According to The European chemical agency [34] a 2-year feed study in rats concluded based
on liver tumors that 2-MI was carcinogenic. A NOAEL of 13 mg/kg bw/day was established. It
was noted that the type of mice used for the study was that is known for its high spontaneous
liver tumor incidence. For that reason it was concluded that the NOAEL was no applicable to
humans. In a follow-up study a NOEAL for reproductive toxicity was established at 2 mg/kg
bw/day.
4-MI
In a study testing imidazoles for their acute conversant eп¬Җects, 4-MI was shown to be the most
potent in rabbits, mice, and chicks at a dose of 360 mg/kg bw/day [35]. The National Toxi-
cology Programme of the US National Institutes of Health (NTP) conducted in 2007 a 2-year
feed study in mice together with mutation assay in S. Typhimurium. From the mutation assay
it was concluded that 4-MI did not show any mutagenic properties. From the 2-year feed study
it was concluded that 4-MI was carcinogenic at high levels of 170mg/kg bw/day in mice. The
combined results from the 2-year feed study and the mutation assay indicate that 4-MI is not
directly carcinogenic but causes cancer via a threshold mechanism (e.g. oxidative stress). It was
concluded that a reasonable NOAEL of 80 mg/kg bw/day could be coincided [36]. EFSA noted
that more consistent data on the carcinogenic eп¬Җects of 4-MI is desirable to establish a more
robust NOAEL.
3.2
Analytical Techniques
The combination of ultra-high-performance liquid chromatography (UHPLC) and tandem mass
spectrometry (MS/MS) is a very powerful analytical method. The technique is used to analyse
organic compounds such as complex biomolecules including proteins and lipids or simpler and
smaller organic molecules such as drugs [37, 38]. A simpliп¬Ғed diagram of the entire analytical
setup can be seen in п¬Ғgure 5 below. In the following sections the major aspects such as the
chromatographic separation, ionization and MS/MS will be described in further detail.
Page 9 of 143
==PAGE:18==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Figure 5: A schematic drawing of the entire analytical setup for HPLC-MS/MS [39].
3.2.1
High-Performance Liquid Chromatography
Chromatographic Column
High-performance liquid chromatography (HPLC) is an analytical technique capable of sepa-
rating organic compounds that are dissolved in a liquid, based on their physical and chemical
properties. The technique utilizes two phases: the stationary phase and the mobile phase. The
stationary phase is the packing material of the chromatographic column whereas the mobile
phase is a mixture of solvents which is pumped through the column.
The separation of compounds are possible do to the retention time (RT) of each compound
which is the time it takes for a compound to elute from the column. This is dependent on the
interaction between the compound, stationary phase and the solvents utilized. A compound
that interact strongly with the packing material of the column will have a longer retention time
compered with a compound that interact less with the packing material. The most utilized
separation mode is reversed-phase chromatography which accounts for 66-75% of all reported
separations [40]. The packing material of a reversed phase column usually consists of porous
silica particles which have a large surface area. The polar surface area of the silica particles are
functionalized by covalently binding diп¬Җerent hydrophobic groups. For reverse phase chromatog-
raphy the most used functionality is the octadecyl carbon chain (C18) [41]. Some examples of
stationary phases for can be seen in п¬Ғgure 6.
Polar compounds will have a short RT on a
reversed-phase column as only little interaction takes place.
Page 10 of 143
==PAGE:19==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Figure 6: Selected examples of commonly used stationary phases in chromatography. Drawn in ChemS-
ketch and inspired by [42].
Reversed-phase chromatography, counter to normal-phase, uses a non-polar stationary phase
and a signiп¬Ғcantly more polar mobile phase (usually aqueous). A comparison between reversed-
phase and normal-phase chromatography can be seen in п¬Ғgure 7. It can be seen that the order
of elution of the compounds is reversed when going from one separation mode to the other.
Figure 7: The diп¬Җerence in stationary phase and mobile phase for (A) reversed-phase chromatography
versus (B) normal phase chromatography [43].
Mobile Phases
For reversed-phase the mobile usually consists of a mixture of water, aqueous volatile acids
or bases and a water miscible organic solvent. Elution can be done isocratically (the water
and organic solvent ratio do not change over time), or with a gradient (the water and organic
solvent ration changes over time). The pH plays an important role in the elution on a reversed-
phase column. Charged compounds are polar and therefore interact less with the hydrophobic
functionalities in the column. The retention of the analytes can therefore be altered by altering
the pH of the mobile phase [44].
Page 11 of 143
==PAGE:20==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
3.2.2
Electron Spray Ionisation
Ionisation is the interface between HPLC and MS/MS. One of the most used techniques is
Electron spray ionisation (ESI) when coupled with LC. The principle behind ESI is that the
compounds separated on the column enters the ionisation source, through a small metal capillary
tube called the inlet capillary. The inlet capillary is contained in the ESI probe. The п¬Ӯow rate
into the metal capillary can vary a lot and can be up to several ВөLВ·minвҲ’1 which is highly
compatible with LC [45]. The inlet capillary is positioned in an atmospheric pressure region
п¬Ғlled with a heated drying gas. At a 90в—Ұangle relative to the ESI probe is the opening leading to
the mass spectrometer (MS) through a low vacuum region and eventually into the high vacuum
region of the mass spectrometer. A diagram of a modern ESI setup can be seen in п¬Ғgure 8.
Figure 8: A diagram of a electron spray ionisation setup [46]
A large voltage, typically between Вұ2.5-6 kV, is applied over the inlet capillary and the inlet
to the MS [47]. This voltage is transferred to the droplets and can either be positive (positive
mode) or negative (negative mode). Depending on the mode positive or negative ions will be
generated. When the high voltage is applied to the capillary a Taylor cone is formed. The
Taylor cone is pulled into a jet which eventually result in a п¬Ғne mist of charged solvent droplets.
A close up image of this formation is shown in п¬Ғgure 9.
Page 12 of 143
==PAGE:21==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Figure 9: A close up image of the formation of a Taylor cone when high voltage is applied to the inlet
capillary in electron spray ionisation [48].
The molecules in the small droplets are now charged with the same charge and therefore they
repel each other as they leave the Taylor cone. In the ion evaporation region the charged droplets
separate to form a п¬Ғne mist. A п¬Ӯow of gas parallel to the inlet capillary is applied to more en-
hance the liquid breaking into a stream of tiny droplets. This gas stream is called the nebulizer
gas. As of now there are two competing theories on how the charges goes from liquid-phase
to gas-phase ions: The ion evaporation mechanism (IEM) and Charged residue model (CRM).
IEM works though the process of solvated ions leaving the larger droplet sequentially and the
droplets shrinks until just desolvated ions remains. In CRM, ions eventually becomes desolvated
as solvent droplets leave the droplet surface. An overview of both of these mechanisms of gener-
ating gas-phase ions can be seen in п¬Ғgure 10. Recent evidence suggest that smaller molecules are
more likely to enter the gas phase via IEM and that larger molecules such as proteins are more
likely to go though the CRM to form multiple charged species. The ionisation method described
is a soft ionisation method which means that the majority of the ions formed correspond to the
intact molecule with minimal fragmentation [45].
Figure 10: A diagram of the two main competing mechanism of generation gas-phase ions from a liquid.
IEM: Ion evaporation mechanism and CRM: Charged residue model [45].
Page 13 of 143
==PAGE:22==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
The most common source of charge is protons. These protons are either generated by the addi-
tion of volatile organic acids to the mobile phase or via reactions happening on the metal/solvent
interface by for example: 2H2O вҶ’4H+ + 4eвҲ’+ O2. It is common that ESI produces ions of
the type [M + zH]z+. Typically, small molecules form single charged ions (z = 1) and multiple
charged species (z вү«1) for bigger molecules such as macromolecules which is consistent with
the above mentioned theories of ion formation.
Diп¬Җerent parameters can be manipulated to achieve higher ionization eп¬ғciency. The parameters
are: Spray Voltage, Probe temperature, probe gas п¬Ӯow, nebulizer gas п¬Ӯow, cone temperature
and cone gas п¬Ӯow. Research done on the subject noted that all the above mentioned parameters
can have a signiп¬Ғcant impact on the ionization eп¬ғciency [49, 50].
3.2.3
Tandem Mass Spectrometry
Tandem mass spectrometry (MS/MS) is a type of instrument build from three quadrupoles
connected in series. The п¬Ғrst and the third quadrupole abbreviated вҖҷQ1вҖҷ and вҖҷQ3вҖҷ, respectively
function as mass analysers while the middle quadrapole abbreviated вҖҷq2вҖҷ serves the purpose of
a collision cell where ions are fragmented. A diagram of a modern MS/MS setup can be seen in
п¬Ғgure 11.
Figure 11: A diagram of a modern tandem mass spectrometry setup with a curved collision cell. Q0:
Ion guide, Q1: The п¬Ғrst quadruple, Q3: The third quadruple [51].
A quadrapole consists of four parallel rods arranged at equal distance from one another around
a central axis. In Q1 and Q3 between the rods a п¬Ӯuctuation п¬Ғeld is created by applying voltages
to the quadrapole rods of both direct current (DC) and alternating current (AC) in the region
of radio frequency (RF). Opposite rods have the same voltages applied. This п¬Ғeld inп¬Ӯuence the
trajectory of the ions traveling though the quadrapole based on their m/z ratio. Each m/z ratio
have optimum RF and DC setting which create a stable trajectory for the ions to travel through
the quadrapole. This means that the voltages applied to the quadrapoles can be programmed
so that only certain m/z ratios are stable through the quadrapole. Ions that do not match the
RF and DC settings to create the a stable trajectory hit the rods and are lost. Quadrapoles
Page 14 of 143
==PAGE:23==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
have two operating modes: Static and scanning. In static mode, both the RF and DC voltage
stays constant which means that only a speciп¬Ғc m/z ratio is let though the quadrapole resulting
in very high selectivity. In scanning mode the RF and DC are changed simultaneous in such a
way so that their ratio stays the same - this lets diп¬Җerent m/z through at diп¬Җerent times and
produces typical mass spectrum [52]. Ions are only let through to the detector when the RF
and DC settings match the m/z ratio to create the stable trajectory of that ion though the
quadrapole. A diagram of a typical quadrapole can be seen in п¬Ғgure 12 [53].
Figure 12: A diagram of a quadrapole. Modiп¬Ғed from: [54]
The voltage applied to q2 is only RF which means that all ions are being let through. This is
because q2 functions as a collision cell and not a mass п¬Ғlter. The function of a collision cell is to
fragment the ions coming from Q1. This is done by placing q2 in a chamber п¬Ғlled with an inert
gas typicality Argon or Hydrogen. The precursor ions coming from Q1 will collide with some
of these gas molecules giving them enough vibrational energy to fragment/dissociate producing
what is called product ions. This process is called collision induced dissociation (CID). The
fragment ions are then let though to Q3 [53, 55].
Both Q1 and Q3 can operate in static and scanning mode which enables the whole MS/MS
instrument to operate in four diп¬ғdent modes: Product ion scan, precursor ion scan, neutral loss
scan and selected reaction monitoring (SRM) also called multiple reaction monitoring (MRM)
which is depicted in п¬Ғgure 13 [55].
Page 15 of 143
==PAGE:24==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Figure 13: Show the four diп¬Җerent modes for an MS/MS instrument. Modiп¬Ғed from: [55]
MRM is the most used mode when it comes to quantiп¬Ғcation of trace compounds in compli-
cated matrices and mixtures because of its selectivity. When using MRM Q1 is set to only let
a selected number of precursor ions into the collision cell. Then Q3 is also set to only monitor
a selected number of product ions. Fragmentation of an analyte may result in the production
of more than one product ion. These ions will be produced in diп¬Җerent amounts but the ratio
between them will stay constant for that analyte. This fact is exploited to increase the selec-
tivity by choosing what is known as quantifying and qualifying ions. Often ions with high m/z
are considered more unique and therefor the risk of interference is lower hence increasing the
selectivity. The ratio between the quantifying and qualifying ions are then used as conп¬Ғrmation
that the observed peak is from the right compound.
An example of quantifying and qualifying ions for 13C6-HMF can be seen in п¬Ғgure 14 and 15
below. In п¬Ғgure 14 both quadrapoles are set to let any thing through with no CID gas. The
mass at 133.2 m/z correspond to the quasi-molecular ion of 13C6-HMF. This precursor ion can
then be chosen to produce the ions seen in п¬Ғgure 15. The MS/MS is set to product ion scan
(see п¬Ғgure 13). The product ions seen in п¬Ғgure 15 are the fragments of the precursor ion with
133.2 m/z. The ratios between the ions 115.0 m/z, 86.1 m/z and 57.4 m/z can then be used as
identity conп¬Ғrmation of this compound since the ratios between them stay the same given that
the collision energy and the CID gas pressure also stays the same.
Page 16 of 143
==PAGE:25==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Figure 14: Ions coming directly from the ion source with out any fragmentation.
Figure 15: The product ions from the precursor ion with 133 m/z. Obtained with a CID ass pressure
of 2 mTorr and a collision energy of 20 eV
3.3
Method Development
3.3.1
Sample Preparation
Salting-out Assisted Liquid-Liquid Extraction
Salting-out assisted liquid-liquid extraction (SALLE) is a technique used to separate miscible
solvents with the use of salts. SALLE is based on the concept that salt molecules dissociate in
water and become charged. As the concentration of salt increases, an increasing amount of water
molecules interact with the charged salt ions. This results in fewer water molecules available
to interact with other solvents such as methanol. This leads to a phase separation between the
aqueous phase and an otherwise miscible solvent. The goal is to extract the target analyte into
the separated organic phase. This approach to sample preparation have gained popularity in
because in can be incorporated into high-throughput LC-MS strategies [56].
Page 17 of 143
==PAGE:26==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Solid Phase Extraction
Solid Phase Extraction (SPE) is a sample puriп¬Ғcation technique that uses modiп¬Ғed solid par-
ticles also called sorbent to separate compounds in a sample based on their chemistry. SPE is
often used to make the subsequent analytical testing more likely to succeed for example by re-
moving interference. Other beneп¬Ғts of SPE include: Sample simpliп¬Ғcation, reduction of matrix
eп¬Җects and concentration trace analytes [57].
Ion-exchange is a type of SPE that is based on the interactions between oppositely charged ions.
The stationary phase particles in ion-exchange chromatography is characterized by having a
charge on their surface which dictates what ions they target. Cation exchanger have a negative
charge and target positively charged ions and vice versa for anion exchange. A diagram of both
of these types of stationary phases can be seen in п¬Ғgure 16 below. On the topic of ion-exchange
SPE a distinction is made between strong and weak ion-exchange. вҖҷStrongвҖҷ indicate that the
sorbent will have the same charge over the entire pH range. This cloud be sulfonic acid for
cation exchange and quaternary ammonium groups for anion exchange вҖҷWeakвҖҷ indicates that
the sorbent can change its charge depending on pH. This could be a carboxylic acid for cationic
exchange and primary, secondary, or tertiary amine groups for anionic exchange [57, 58].
Figure 16:
Shows the two types of stationary phases for ion-exchange SPE and how they attract
oppositely charged ions [57]
Strong cationic exchange can be used when working with analytes that are weak bases and
therefore, can change charge from neutral to positive depending on pH. In strong cationic ex-
change the packing material have permanently negatively charged groups such as sulfonic acid
(SOвҲ’
3 ) which can trap positively charged ions. Imidazoles have a pKa of вҲј7 and can be pro-
tonated/deprotonated on the pyrrole type nitrogen (See п¬Ғgure 17 below). Therefore cationic
exchange SPE is a good choice of SPE to use for these types of compounds [57].
Page 18 of 143
==PAGE:27==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Figure 17: Shows the protonation of a simple imidazole ring. The pyrrole nitrogen have a lone pair
which participate in the aromaticity of the imidazole ring and is therefore not available to protonation.
The imidazole nitrogen have a lone pair too which points out of the ring and is therefore available for
protonation.
Pre-treatment of the the sample with an acid is necessary before running the SPE. The acid
will protonate the analyte which then can be trapped by the negatively charged groups on the
packing material in the SEP column. At the pH = pKa for the analyte means that 50% of the
analyte will be ionized and 50% will be un-ionized. For that reason the sample pH should be
adjusted approximately two units below the pKa of the analyte of interest for cation exchange
[57, 58]. When eluting the sorbent and analyte is treated with a base which removes the pos-
itive charge on the analyte and thereby also removes the interaction between the sorbent and
the analyte which result in elution [58].
Ion-exchange chromatography is a type of capture strategy where the analyte of interest is
captured (via ionic interactions) and kept in the sorbent until elution takes place. A general
workп¬Ӯow for this strategy can be seen in п¬Ғgure 18 below.
Figure 18: The п¬Ғve overall steps in a SPE procedure. Modiп¬Ғed from [57]
Page 19 of 143
==PAGE:28==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
3.3.2
Statistical Design of Experiments
Many processes, like ionisation in the case of HPLC-MS/MS, have a number of important factors
or critical process parameters (CPPвҖҷs) that have a large inп¬Ӯuence on how eп¬ғciently the process
runs [49]. It can be diп¬ғcult and time consuming to п¬Ғnd the optimal operating conditions or
settings. Rather than using a "one factor at a time" approach which in the majority of cases fail
to improve the process signiп¬Ғcantly the design of experiment methodology can be applied. The
drawback of the "one factor at a time" approach is that it overlooks the interaction of factors,
also called two-way interactions, this occurs when the eп¬Җect of one factor on the response is
dependent on the level of another factor. Statistical design of experiments abbreviated DOE is
a way to pre-plan experiments to obtain the most amount of information about the process in
question with the least amount of runs or tests [59]. These well planned experimental designs
make it possible to analyze the obtained data to yield objective and valid conclusions. DOE can
both be used to screen out the most important factors or produce a model which then can be
used to optimise settings for the factors, often both.
DOE is a multi varied approach in which more factors are changed simultaneously. Factors are
varied from their lowest to their highest values normally coded -1 and 1, respectively. In the
case of a three factor DOE the factors can be depicted in a cube called the design space as seen
in п¬Ғgure 19. Each blue corner represents a set of test conditions and the red dot represent a set
of test conditions which lies in the center of the design space where all factors take the value in
the middle of the test range.
Figure 19: A design space with a center point for a 3 factor design.
If the response behave like in п¬Ғgure 20A (linear) a two-level experimental design without center
points is adequate. If the response behaves like the one in 20B with curvature in the response
surface (quadratic) a two-level design without center points will not be able to detect quadratic
eп¬Җects nor model the response.
Page 20 of 143
==PAGE:29==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Center point can be included in the experimental design to detect quadratic eп¬Җects but the
design is still not able to п¬Ғt a model. Therefore, if nothing is known about the process in ques-
tion and the experimental budget allows, center points can be a wise investment. Furthermore,
replicate runs can add precision to some estimates and add more certainty in the вҖҷlack of п¬Ғt testвҖҷ.
Figure 20: (A) Linear eп¬Җects and (B) quadratic eп¬Җect.
Full Factorial Design
A use full type of design is the Full factorial design. In this type of design all combinations of
high and low for all factors are tested together so 2Numberoffactors (not counting replicate runs
and center points). A full factorial design with 3 factors would require 23 experiments and have
a design space similar to the one in п¬Ғgure 19 except for the center point. The design table for
this type of experimental design can be seen in table 3 below.
Table 3: A 23 two-level, full factorial design table.

A full factorial design allows for the estimation of all possible interactions. With many factors
a full factorial design is unpractical and is typically only used when one have a smaller amount
of factors. Other designs such as Plackett-Burman or fractional factorial designs can be used as
screening designs to п¬Ғnd the most inп¬Ӯuential factors [49].
3.4
Method Validation
A method validation is performed to showcase the reliability of an analytical method and is
therefore an essential activity to ensure that well grounded results are obtained. A validation
should be carried out for all analytical methods but as the sophistication and complexity of the
applied technique increases often more work is required and more challenges may arise. Simi-
larly, issues may be encountered when working with a complex matrix. HPLC-MS/MS is a very
powerful technique that oп¬Җers a great sensitivity and speciп¬Ғcity towards the target analyte, but
Page 21 of 143
==PAGE:30==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
it is often challenged on its robustness. For this reason it is of the utmost importance to deter-
mine the capability and limitations of the method in question so that it works as expected under
intended use and results can be trusted [60]. For a method to be accredited is must comply with
requirements provided by ISO/IEC 17025 [61]. A validation will normally include considerations
regarding the following parameters: Speciп¬Ғcity/selectivity, linearity and sensitivity, precision,
trueness, Limit of Detection (LOD), Limit of Quantiп¬Ғcation (LOQ) and robustness/ruggedness
[62, 63]. These parameters will be described in greater details in the materials and methods
section (subsection 4.8 Method validation). The extend of the method validation, that is which
parameters to include, depends on the purpose of the method, analytical feasibility and can be
reduced if the method is already validated for another matrix or in another laboratory [63].
Page 22 of 143
==PAGE:31==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021

4.1
Project Workп¬Ӯow and Overview
The project comprised a range of activities which can be grouped as follows: (1) Activities per-
formed as a part of the method development for the analysis of HMF, THI, 2-MI and 4-MI in
caramel colour; (2) preliminary tests carried out as an extension to the method validation; and
(3) method validation and activities performed to express the concentration of the compounds
on a equivalent colour basis, to assess the manufacturing conditions contribution to the forma-
tion of the substance, and to evaluate the human exposure to the compounds. The laboratory
activities were performed in a simultaneous fashion, and the п¬Ӯowchart seen in п¬Ғgure 21 below
summarise the project and gives a more detailed overview. Since HMF was expected in signiп¬Ғ-
cant higher quantities than that of the imidazoles and diп¬Җerent sample preparation procedures

Spring 2021
were required, the compounds were divided into two categories: Category 1 being HMF alone
and category 2 comprising 2-MI, 4-MI and THI.
Figure 21: The overall project workп¬Ӯow including preliminary activities carried out as a part of the

The same UHPLC-MS/MS system and gradient programme were employed for both categories
and an overview of relevant settings can be seen in table 8.

Thermo Scientiп¬Ғc coupled to an EVOQ Elite Triple Quadrupole Mass Spectrometer from Bruker.
A Waters ACQUITY UPLCВ®CSH в„ўC18 column with the dimensions, 2.1 x 100 mm and a
particle size of 1.7 Вөm was attached to a Waters VanGuardв„ўPre-Column with the dimensions
2.1 x 5 mm. The mobile phases were composed of 5 mM ammonia in Milli-Q water as eluent A
and pure methanol as eluent B. As depicted in п¬Ғgure 22, the gradient programme was a follows:
An initial composition of 5% B was kept for 2 minutes and then linearly raised to 30% over
the period of 2.5 minutes. In a matter of 0.1 minutes the composition was then brought back
to the initial 5% B. Finally, it was set to equilibrate for 5.2 minutes so that a chromatographic
run time of 10 minutes was obtained, see п¬Ғgure 22. The preparation of eluent A can be seen in
appendix C.1.

4.3
Preparation of Stock Solutions
Individual stock solutions for all compounds were prepared in Milli-Q water.
For the non-
isotopically labelled compounds Uracil, HMF, 2-MI and 4-MI individual stock solutions of 1
mg/mL were prepared and 0.1 mg/mL for THI. For the isotope labelled compounds a stock
solutions of 1 mg/mL were prepared for 4-MI-d3 whereas individual stock solutions of 0.1 mg/L
were prepared for 13C6-HMF and 13C6-THI. Working solutions containing either individual or a
mixture of compounds were prepared in the range of 0.1 Вөg/mL to 10 Вөg/mL for non-isotopically
labelled compounds and internal standards. A 1000 ng/mL solution of Uracil in Milli-Q water
was prepared to determine the dead volume. All solutions were stored at 6 в—ҰC.
4.4
Test Samples and Manufacturer Samples
A total of 42 samples were received from the company Spezialzucker-Raп¬ғnerie Lage GmbH &
Co. Betriebs-KG [64]. Three of them constituted larger bottles (one of E150a, E150c and E150d)
which were available from the beginning of the project. These will from now on be denoted as
test samples and were used for development and validation purposes. The remaining 39 samples
(four of the type E150a, 28 of E150c and seven of E150d) were received on an ongoing basis and
will be referred as manufacturer samples since they were taken at diп¬Җerent steps in the manu-
facturing process. These were analyse to access the manufacturing conditions inп¬Ӯuence on the
constituent levels. The manufacturer samples were labelled according to batch no. and the step
Page 27 of 143
==PAGE:36==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
at which they were sampled in the manufacturing process e.g sample 1.2 is from the п¬Ғrst batch
and constitute the second sample. Figure 23 illustrate the production pathways for the man-
ufacturer samples and show to which class each batch belong. More details regarding process
conditions can be seen in appendix E. Raw samples were stored in the refrigerator at 6 в—ҰC until
further use and working solutions of each sample were prepared at a concentration of 1 mg/mL.
The working solutions were prepared by transferring 100 mg of sample to a 100 mL volumetric
п¬Ӯask and diluted to volume with Milli-Q water. Working solutions were used for all experiments
with the exception of the method validations. All working solutions were likewise stored at 6 в—ҰC.

4.5
Extended Method Validation
4.5.1
Stability Study
A stability study was conducted for HMF, 2-MI and 4-MI over a period of six weeks. Four
diп¬Җerent storage conditions were investigated: at room temperature in light and dark, at 6 в—ҰC
and at вҲ’20 в—ҰC. At all storage conditions two concentrations were chosen: 10 ng/mL and 100
ng/mL. Each week a freshly made standard at both concentration levels were analysed together
with the old standards kept at the diп¬Җerent storage conditions. Five replicates were analysed for
each standard. A t-test was performed to compare the freshly made standard with old standards
kept at diп¬Җerent storage conditions, to see if they were signiп¬Ғcantly diп¬Җerent from one another.
4.5.2
Matrix Eп¬Җects
The extend of matrix eп¬Җects were evaluated by preparing two calibration curves for each com-
pound, one in Milli-Q water and one in matrix.
Diп¬Җerences in the slopes between the two
calibration curves without IS indicate matrix eп¬Җects. To evaluate if the use of IS could com-
pensate for potential matrix eп¬Җects, the two calibration curves were also compared with IS. To
compare the slopes, a t-test was performed in Excel with the function вҖҷSlopeTestвҖҷ from the re-
source pack вҖҷXrealstatsвҖҷ. All calibration curves comprised the following concentration levels: 0,
Page 28 of 143
==PAGE:37==

Spring 2021

to make the calibration curves in matrix. Aside from spiking levels, the matrix-containing cal-
ibration curves were prepared by following the procedures described in section 4.7.1. The one
for HMF followed dilution procedure 2 for category 1, see table 10 for the spiking amounts of
HMF and IS. For the imidazoles the calibration curve acquired in matrix was made by following
the dilution and SPE procedure for category 2 described in section 4.7.1, see table 11 for the

4.5.3
Screening for HMF and 4-MI
Blank samples were only available for THI and 2-MI hence, samples were analysed to п¬Ғnd the
ones containing the lowest amount of HMF and 4-MI to be used in the method validation.
The three test samples were screened for 4-MI and all manufacturer samples of the type E150c
were screened for HMF. The screening for 4-MI was only performed on test samples since few
SPE columns were available at the time, and likewise, screening for HMF was only performed
on E150c manufacturer samples since batches of other classes were not received yet. In spite
of that, the sample containing the lowest content of HMF was believed to be found among
the E150c caramel colours based on constituent levels reported by Vollmuth [1]. Screening for
HMF followed the dilution procedure 2 described for category 1 whereas the screening for 4-MI
followed the procedure for category 2, both described in section 4.7.1.

4.7
Quantiп¬Ғcation of 2-MI, 4-MI, HMF and THI
4.7.1
Sample Preparation
Category 1
Vollmuth [1] reported concentrations of HMF varying from 10-33700 mg/kg across the four types
of caramel colour. To cover this expected range two dilution procedures were established for the
determination of HMF, namely a 5 mg/L and a 50 mg/L of caramel colour in water designated
dilution procedure 1 and 2, respectively.
To spare the instrument, samples were п¬Ғrst taken
through the п¬Ғrst dilution procedure and analysed. For samples containing a quantity below the
level of quantiп¬Ғcation when using procedure 1, the second procedure should be applied. Both
procedures constituted two dilution steps and since a limited amount of 13C6-HMF was avail-
able, the internal standard (IS) was added in the second step. IS was added so that a п¬Ғnale
concentration of 10 ng/mL was obtained.
For both procedures, 100 mg of caramel colour was transferred to a 100 mL volumetric п¬Ӯask and
п¬Ғlled to volume with Milli-Q water after which it was thoroughly mixed. Following procedure
1, 500 ВөL of the sample mix and 0.1 mL of IS (1000 ng/mL) were transferred to a 100 mL
volumetric п¬Ӯask and п¬Ғlled to volume with Milli-Q water. Following procedure 2, 500 ВөL of the
sample mix and 1 mL of IS (100 ng/mL) were transferred to a 10 mL volumetric п¬Ӯask and
likewise п¬Ғlled to volume. After thorough mixing 250 ВөL of the dilution was then п¬Ғltered through
a Mini-UniPrepTM syringeless п¬Ғlter with a pore size of 0.2 Вөm.
Page 30 of 143
==PAGE:39==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Category 2
Prior to the sample dilution, 1250 ВөL of the internal standards 13C6-THI (for THI) and 4-MI-d3
(for 2-MI and 4-MI), both with a concentration of 1000 ng/mL were added to 100 mg sample in a
weighing boat (to achieve a п¬Ғnal concentration of IS fo 25 ng/mL) and gently mixed with a glass
spatula. The isotope labelled compounds were added to compensate for potential matrix eп¬Җects
and gently mixed with a glass spatula again. The mix was then put to rest for 30 minutes after
which it was transferred to a 100 mL volumetric п¬Ӯasks and brought to volume with Milli-Q water.
A volume of 500 ВөL of the diluted sample (1000 mg/L) and 20 ВөL 0.1 M HCL were added
to a volumetric п¬Ӯask and brought to 3 mL with Milli-Q water. A SPE column of the type
StrataTM-X-C 33 Вөm Polymeric Strong Cation was conditioned with 2 mL of methanol followed
by activation with 2 mL 0.1 M HCl and then loading with the 3 mL sample mix. To ensure
ionic exchange, the prepared sample was slowly passed through the cartridge after which it was
washed with 2 mL of methanol. The elution was carried out with 4 mL of a 1.5% ammonium
hydroxide in methanol. The elute was evaporated to dryness under a gentle stream of Nitrogen
(2 L/min) at 40 в—ҰC. The dry extract was dissolved in 250 ВөL of Milli-Q water. The extract was
п¬Ғltered through a 0.2 Вөm Mini-UniPrepTM syringeless п¬Ғlter vial and analysed.
4.8
Method Validation
The method validation was conducted in three series, all carried out on diп¬Җerent days and per-
formed by two people (series 1 and 2 by the same operator). Two methods were established for
the analysis of the four compounds and two distinct validation categories were correspondingly
chosen: Category 1 being HMF alone and category 2 comprising 2-MI, 4-MI and THI. The
validation setup applicable to both categories can be seen in table 13 with the selected number
of spiking levels and determinations which comply with the requirements listed in the NMKL
procedure no. 4, version 3 (2009) for validation of chemical analytical methods [65] and the
Standard Operating Procedure (SOP) K-KVA-014, employed internally by the department of
Analytical Food Chemistry at DTU Food [62]. The SOP serves to secure a streamlined pro-
cedure, adequate for validation of analytical methods used for food. The method validation
included assessment of the following validation parameters: Speciп¬Ғcity/selectivity, linearity and
sensitivity, precision, trueness, LOD, LOQ and robustness/ruggedness.
Page 31 of 143
==PAGE:40==

Spring 2021
Table 13: The Validation protocol with the selected number of series, spiking levels and determinations.
The spiking levels 1, 2 and 3 being no spike, 300 and 35000 mg/kg for HMF; 1.25, 5 and 10 mg/kg for
THI; 1.25, 10 and 140 mg/kg for 2-MI and no spike, 10 and 140 mg/kg for 4-MI. The replicates used
to assess the precision (spiking level 1) were also used to determine the Limit of Detection (LOD) and
Limit of Quantiп¬Ғcation (LOQ). Additionally, to prove that a blank matrix was used for THI and 2-MI,
unspiked samples were analysed (п¬Ғve replicates for each series).

No blank samples were available for HMF and 4-MI. Thus, the lowest spiking level for these
compounds constituted the natural content of the samples containing the lowest amounts. The
spiking levels were selected to imitate the expected range of the compounds based on the caramel
colour safety update by Vollmuth [1]. The safety update did not provide any constituents levels
for 2-MI. So for simplicity, the same spiking levels were chosen for 2-MI as for 4-MI with the
exception of the lowest level. Spiking level 1, 2 and 3 were no spike, 300 mg/kg and 35000
mg/kg for HMF; 1.25 mg/kg, 5 mg/kg and 10 mg/kg for THI; 1.25 mg/kg, 10 mg/kg and 140
mg/kg for 2-MI, and no spike, 10 mg/kg and 140 mg/kg for 4-MI, respectively. in addition, the
caramel colour used as a blank sample For THI and 2-MI were analysed п¬Ғve times to ensure
that the sample was in fact a blank sample.
Speciп¬Ғcity/Selectivity
Selectivity can be deп¬Ғned as the ability of the method to speciп¬Ғcally and precisely determine
the target analyte in a certain matrix.
This means that it should be able distinguish the
response obtained for the target analyte and the response from interfering compounds present
in the sample. The selectivity of the methods were assessed by evaluating the chromatograpic
separation of the compounds. Based on literature (Wang and Schnute [16], Schlee et al. [13])
peak tailing of 2-MI and 4-MI were expected. Thus, to ensure proper separation of compounds
the peak resolutions (RS) were calculated according to the IUPAC deп¬Ғnition (equation 1),
where peak widths are measured at the baseline [66], see п¬Ғgure 24. RS>2 should be obtained
in compliance with guidelines issued by FDA [60, 67].
RS = 2 В· (RTB вҲ’RTA)
(WB + WA)
(1)
Where:
RTA = Retention time of compound A.
RTB = Retention time of compound B.
WA = Average peak width at base for compound A.
WB = Average peak width at base for compound B.

In addition to resolution the product ion ratio is a feature used as identity conп¬Ғrmation. When
using MS/MS a quantiп¬Ғer and qualiп¬Ғer ion is often chosen. The qualiп¬Ғer ion is used to quantify
the amount of analyte in the sample. The qualiп¬Ғer ions is used to conп¬Ғrm that the observed
quantiп¬Ғer belongs to the analyte in question and not matrix. For a given analyte in standard
the ration between quantiп¬Ғer and qualiп¬Ғer stays constant. The ion ratio is calculated as the
peak area (or intensity) between the smallest area over the biggest area. A reference ion ratio
is calculated from the ion ratios obtained from calibration curves. Tolerances for ion ratios are
put forward by 2002/657/EC which can be seen in table 14 [68].

Linearity and Sensitivity
To asses linearity when using a calibration curve for quantiп¬Ғcation, the calibration curve should
consist of at least п¬Ғve levels evenly distributed in the relevant concentration range. If the method
should mainly be used for lower concentrations, additional levels should be included in the lower
region of the calibration curve. Scatter plots with individual determinations were set up and the
method of least squares were used to determine the lines of best п¬Ғt. Assessment of the linearity
included visual evaluation of the calibration curves, the residual plots as well as evaluation of
the obtained R-square values (R2). The signiп¬Ғcance of the linearity was determined by a F-test
and the sensitivity was calculated as the slopes of the calibration curves.
Precision
Precision covers the terms Repeatability, In-house reproducibility and Reproducibility, and can
be expressed as a Coeп¬ғcient of Variation (CV%). For concentrations вүҘ1000 Вөg/kg the CV%
should not exceed 16%.
Repeatability (Sr) is a measure of вҖҷthe closeness of agreement between independently obtained
test resultsвҖҷ acquired by the same operator using the same equipment, in the same laboratory,
and within a short period of time (same day) [69]. Sr should be determined with a minimum of
six degrees of freedom and is calculated by the use of equation 3 [62].
The In-house reproducibility (SR,Intra) is a measure of within-laboratory variation and can be
explained as the variation between independent test results obtained when diп¬Җerent operators
use the same equipment, in the same laboratory and on diп¬Җerent days [70]. SR,Intra should
be obtained from at least three series and is found by using equation 4 in accordance with
ISO 5725-2 (2019) [71]. Reproducibility explains the variation between laboratories to ensure
that same results can be obtained regardless of which laboratory the analysis is performed in.
Reproducibility was excluded from this validation in compliance with the guidelines for single-
laboratory validations [72]. Precision was expressed as CV% by the use of equation 5.

Trueness
Trueness describes systematic errors/bias causing obtained test results to deviate in a certain
manner from the true value. The term can therefore be deп¬Ғned as вҖҷthe closeness of agreement
between the average value acquired from a great number of test results and the value accepted
as the referenceвҖҷ. Trueness was assessed in terms of recovery of fortiп¬Ғed samples being the lowest
spiking level in the validation. The recovery percentages (R%) were found by the use of equation
6. According to the NMKL Procedure No. 4 the R% of fortiп¬Ғed samples with concentrations
вүҘ100 Вөg/kg must lie within the range of 70-110% [65].

QS

QO = The quantity of analyte naturally present in the sample (without spike).

Limit of detection (LOD) and limit of quantiп¬Ғcation (LOQ) are the lowest amounts of the target
analyte present in the matrix that is possible to distinguish from zero and that is possible to
quantify with a satisfactory precision and accuracy, respectively. The LOD and LOQ of each
compound were determined based on the lowest spiking level in the method validation and equa-
tion 7 below was used to estimate both limits. A minimum requirement of 6 determinations
should be included in the calculation of these limits [62].

Ruggedness
Ruggedness also termed robustness describes the capability of the method to deliver precise
results when small changes occur [62]. Changes could be with respect to the matrix in question,
the analysis conditions and the sample preparation procedure etc. Common incidents such as
temporary equipment issues, lack of reagents and sickness can result in delays causing that
samples cannot be analysed on the same day as they were prepared.
For this reason, the
methods resistance to such delays was investigated by re-injecting already analysed samples
and their associated calibration curve after a period of 14 days for HMF and 40 days for the
imidazoles. For all four compounds, the second spiking level in the п¬Ғrst validation series was
used for this purpose. These were stored at 6 в—ҰC.
4.9

In terms of regulation, maximum levels for THI and 4-MI are expressed on an equivalent colour
basis (see equation 9). The colour equivalent basis and the variables required to calculate it
were determined in accordance with the guidelines stated in the speciп¬Ғcation prepared at the
74th JECFA meeting (2011) and published in FAO JECFA Monographs 11 (2011) [12]. Only one
exception being that the compounds were quantiп¬Ғed by the use of HPLC-MS/MS rather than
HPLC-UV for THI and GasвҖ“liquid chromatography (GLC) for 4-MI. Firstly, the concentrations
of the compounds were expressed on at solids basis (CS) by the use of equation 10 and then on
a colour equivalent basis.

Determination of Solids Content
1.5-2 g of caramel colour and 30 g of sea sand (Silicon dioxide puriп¬Ғed by acid and calcined)
were transferred to a foil tray, and carefully mixed with a glass spatula until a homogeneous
appearance was reached. To calculate the %solids, the accurate weight of each component was
measured prior to drying and for particularly solid caramel colours a few droplets of Milli-Q were
afterwards added directly unto the caramel to ease the mixing step. The sample mix including
spatula was then pre-dried in an incubator at 60 в—ҰC for at least 24 hours and dried in a vacuum
oven at 50 в—ҰC until a stable weight was obtained. Before weighing, the samples were placed in
a desiccator and cooled to room temperature. The %solids could then be found by the use of

4.10
Exposure Evaluation
Margin of exposure (MOE) is a tool used to assess whether an exposure from food to a given
substance is harmful or not. MOE is the ratio between NOAEL for a given endpoint e.g. cancer
and the populations exposure to the chemical or a substance. The NOAEL value is typically
obtained through animal toxicology studies. The formula for MOE can be seen in equation
13. Note that MOE is unit less and therefore NOAEL and the exposure must have the same
units (e.g. [mg/kg bw/day]). MOE is usually used for compounds that are both genotoxic and
carcinogenic but can also be used to give an indication of risk for other undesirable eп¬Җects.
MOE = NOAEL
Exposure
(13)
For substances that are not genotoxic and carcinogenic a MOE of вүҘ100 is considered of low
health risk. A higher threshold for MOE might be used in cases where no chronic exposure data
is available, there is uncertainties regarding the study design or the quality of date which upon
NOAEL is based, it is probable that the exposure will be under estimated for example if food
consumption data is limited or that a special population group is considered for example pregnant
or children. If a substances is genotoxic and carcinogenic a MOE of вүҘ10000 is considered of low
health risk.

==PAGE:46==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS

5
Results and Discussion
5.1
Method Development
The overall aim of the method development was to:
вҖў Obtain a good separation between the analytes of interest and other interfering compounds.
Preferably with a resolution вүҘ2.
вҖў Find and optimise a proper extraction method for the imidazoles.
вҖў Optimise ion source parameters with respect to THI.
5.1.1
Separation Method
All tests during the development of the separation method were carried out with pure com-
pounds in Milli-Q water at a concentration of 100 ng/ml, and the aim was obtain a larger
retention time for THI in order to separate it from non-retaining compounds. Goscinny et al.
[4] reported a retention time of 1.35 minutes for THI on a C18 column and described that THI
eluted with all the polar compounds. This resulted in a "hilly" peak and made quantiп¬Ғcation
diп¬ғcult. Preferably THI should have a retention time above two minutes. Furthermore, a good
separation between 2-MI and 4-MI was an important aspect since they have the same mass and
almost identical fragmentation patterns. This means they cannot be distinguished via mass
spectrometry alone and should therefore be separated via chromatography. The analysis time
should not be too short due to the aforementioned reasons but at the same time should be kept
as short as possible, preferably вүӨ10 minutes. The work of Goscinny et al. [4] and Wang and
Schnute [16] was used as a starting point for the development.
Preliminary Setup
The column used as a starting point constituted a C30 reversed phase column (2.1 x 150 mm, 3
Вөm particle size). It was quickly observed, based on the retention time of the compounds (HMF
eluted before 2-MI and 4-MI), that the column was damaged in some way. A chromatogram
obtained from the use of the C30 column can be seen in п¬Ғgure 51 in appendix H.1. It was
not possible to recover the column by washing and in lack of another C30 column, the work
proceeded with the use of newly purchased C18 reversed phase column.
Initial Settings
The п¬Ғrst conп¬Ғguration of parameters was as follows: C18 reversed phase column (2.1 x 100
mm, 1.7 Вөm particle size) was used with mobile phase A being Milli-Q water containing 5 mM
ammonium hydroxide solution and mobile phase B being methanol. The elution was done with
a gradient program which started at 5% B for the п¬Ғrst two minutes. This was linearly increased
to 30% B over the course of 2.5 minutes. The conditions was then reset in 0.1 minutes and held
for 3.6 minutes to make the total analysis time 6 minutes. The mobile phase was delivered at a
п¬Ӯow rate of 0.3 mL/min with the column temperature at 25 в—ҰC. A chromatogram from the п¬Ғrst
HPLC settings can be seen in п¬Ғgure 25.
Page 38 of 143
==PAGE:47==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Figure 25: Chromatogram acquired by using original settings as in Goscinny et al. [4]. Brown peak:
THI (1.66 min); Green and red peaks to the left: 2-MI (2.87 min); Green and red peaks to the right:
4-MI (4.12 min); and blue peak: HMF (5.57 min).
The retention time for THI using these settings was below 2 (1.66) minuets, as expected based
on Goscinny et al. [4]. To determine if unretained compounds would interfere with THI a stan-
dard of Uracil was analysed as this compound does not have any retention on a C18 column.
Uracil is known as a void marker and can be used to approximate the dead volume [73]. Uracil
had a retention time of 1.5 minutes which is very close to that of THI. It might be a problem
that unretained compounds from the caramel matrix would co-elute with THI.
Optimisation of THI, 2-MI and 4-MI Retention
THI is very polar with a CLogP of -2.7 (as seen in table 2). To increase the retention time the
organic part of the mobile phase was increased. To do this the elution was changed to isocratic
with 10% B. Furthermore the pressure of the instrument was reaching above 650 bars which
resulted in frequent leaks. Therefore going forward the п¬Ӯow rate was lowered from 0.3 mL/min
to 0.2 mL/min. With the lower п¬Ӯow rate the pressure did not exceed 350 bars. The changes
improved the retention time of THI to 1.87 minutes but also resulted in peak broadening in
general and therefore, a low resolution between 2-MI and 4-MI of 1.17 was observed, see table
15. It was speculated that the lower п¬Ӯow rate was resulting in the broadening of the peaks. A
chromatogram can be seen in п¬Ғgure 52 in appendix H.1.
Optimisation of eluent programme
The п¬Ӯow rate was set back to 0.3 mL/min while the column temperature was increased to 40
в—ҰC. The hope was that the higher temperature would compensate for the increase in п¬Ӯow rate
and keep the pressure in an acceptable range. This was not the case and a п¬Ӯow rate of 0.25
mL/min was tested but the pressure was still above 600 bar. To improve the resolution of 2-MI
and 4-MI the gradient was introduced again as described in the previous section.
Page 39 of 143
==PAGE:48==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Final Adjustment of Flow Rate
The column temperature was kept at 40 в—ҰC while the п¬Ӯow rate was lowered to 0.2 mL/min
again. At this point the settings were: C18 reversed phase column (2.1 x 100 mm, 1.7 Вөm
particle size). Elution was carried out with the following mobile phases: Mobile phase A was
Milli-Q containing 5mM ammonium hydroxide solution and mobile phase B was methanol. The
elution was done with the gradient described above. The mobile phase was delivered at a п¬Ӯow
rate of 0.2 mL/min at a column of 40 в—ҰC. With these settings satisfactory results were obtained
in terms of a resolution >2 between all target analytes and a retention time for THI at 2
minutes. A chromatogram can be seen in п¬Ғgure 26. Furthermore the resolutions and retention
time for all compounds and optimisation runs can be seen in table 15 and 16, respectively (raw
data can be seen in table 40 in appendix H.1). In conclusion, an improved resolution between
the compounds as well as a prolonged retention time for THI were obtained. Thus, the above
described conditions were used as the п¬Ғnal separation method.

Green and red peaks to the left: 2-MI (3.05 min); Green and red peaks to the right: 4-MI (4.23 min);
and blue peak: HMF (5.25 min).

5.1.2
Sample Preparation using Solid Phase Extraction
The sample preparation using SPE was based on the procedures described by Goscinny et al.
[4] and Jacobs et al. [74] from which several trials were developed. The description of all trials
can be seen in appendix F table 39. The two starting approaches regarding the extraction were
very similar. Goscinny et al. [4] approach was as follows: Condition with 2 mL of methanol
to solvate the material in the SPE cartridge, next equilibration was done with 2 mL of 0.1 M
HCL to remove the organic solvent to make the material able to interact with the sample, the
acidiп¬Ғed sample was loaded (acidiп¬Ғcation is needed and is explained in section 3.3.1). A wash
was then performed with 2 mL of methanol after which the elution was carried out with 4 mL
of a 2% (v/v) ammonium hydroxide in methanol. Jacobs et al. [74] conditioned with 3 mL of
methanol, equilibrated with 3 mL of Milli-Q, washed with 3 mL of a 2 M solution of formic acid
in water and 3 mL of methanol after which the elution was carried out with 4 mL of a 5% (v/v)
ammonium hydroxide in methanol. The extra washing step with acid was included because low
or high pH can facilitate removal of interference. High pH in the washing step cannot be used
in this case because the ionic interaction between the analyte and the cartridge material would
cease and the analyte would elute. The chromatograms for Jacobs et al. [74] and Goscinny et al.
[4] without change can be seen in п¬Ғgure 27 referred to as trial 1 and 2, respectively. The peak
for THI and 4-MI is acceptable but the peak for 2-MI has a low intensity and could be improved.
A mix of the two starting approaches was made by using the activation step from Jacobs et al.
[74] while the rest was kept as Goscinny et al. [4] (Trial 3 in п¬Ғgure 27). The chromatogram did
not change for 2-MI and 4-MI while being worse for THI compered to trial 1 and 2. A very
"jittering" peak was obtained for THI which was not satisfactory in terms of peak shape.
When eluting, it was observed that using 5% (v/v) ammonium hydroxide in methanol resulted
in a darker coloured eluate compared to using only 2% (v/v) ammonium hydroxide in methanol.
Based on the observations four new trials were made by changing the concentration of ammo-
nium hydroxide in methanol to 1% (v/v) and 1.5% (v/v) for the two starting approaches (Trial
4-7).
Trial 4 was based on the procedure from Goscinny et al.
[4] with elution done with 1.5%
ammonium hydroxide in methanol and showed the best results for all three compounds. Trial 4
only had one simple washing step with 2 mL of methanol. With inspiration from Jacobs et al.
[74] the washing step of trial 4 was changed to 3 mL of a 2 M solution of formic acid followed by
a wash with 3 mL of methanol while elution was done with 2% (v/v) ammonium hydroxide in
methanol. This was done to try to remove more matrix from the carriage before eluting (Trial
8). This did not improve the peaks for 2-MI and THI. A last trial was made with the same
conditions as trial 8 but elution was done with 1.5% (v/v) ammonium hydroxide in methanol
(Trial 9). Trial 9 did improve the peak for THI to some degree but not compared to trial 4.
The best result was obtained with trial 4 based on the peak shape of THI and the intensity and
peak shape for 2-MI. Therefore these conditions were used for the SPE procedure in the п¬Ғnal
method.

==PAGE:50==
