5.1.3
Salting-out Assisted Liquid-Liquid Extraction
As an alternative to SPE a liquid-liquid extraction method was employed. At п¬Ғrst, Feng et al.
[75] was used as a source of inspiration, but since chlorinated solvents could not be applied in
DTUвҖҷs laboratories due to safety precautions, another approach was sought. The starting point
then constituted the salting-out assisted liquid-liquid extraction (SALLE) methods described by
Xie et al. [76] in which the extraction systems comprised ethanol and water. Instead of ethanol,
a п¬Ғrst trial was carried out with methanol since the lower boiling point of methanol makes it
easy to evaporate, hence made it possible to concentrate the sample prior to analysis.
As a п¬Ғrst trial 2 mL of methanol was mixed with 2 mL of Milli-Q water and 1 g of K2HPO4
to see if a phase separation took place. K2HPO4 was used because Feng et al. [75] found this
salt to yield the best result. Since a phase separation was achieved further trials were carried
out with use of sample dilutions and where the composition was changed. The total of eight
SALLE trials can be seen in table 38 in appendix F. For all trials, the salt was thoroughly mixed
with the liquids and a clear phase separation took place within a few minutes of shaking. To
spare the instrument, the colour of the extracts was used as an indication of how much matrix
was removed. Accordingly, only the brightest extract was analysed. The brightest extract was
obtained by the use of trial 8 where 2 mL of methanol was mixed with 2 mL of a 0.1% sample
dilution (caramel colour in Milli-Q water), 3 g of K2HPO4 and 40 ВөL of 25% ammonium hy-
droxide. This was the only trial where ammonium hydroxide was applied. The organic phase
(upper layer) was isolated and evaporated to dryness. The dry extract was then dissolved in
250 ВөL of Milli-Q water and analysed. The chromatogram for THI, 2-MI and 4-MI can be seen
in п¬Ғgure 28 below. As shown, no peaks developed at the expected retention time for THI which
may be explained by the very polar properties of THI causing it to stay in aqueous phase during
the phase separation. Hence, only peaks from other compounds stemming the matrix are devel-
oped. A small peak can be seen for 2-MI and 4-MI but a interfering peak is present for 4-MI.
Since no peak was developed at the expected retention time for THI, a co-eluting substance was
interfering with 4-MI and a desirable result was obtained with SPE, the potential of SALLE was
not further explored.
Page 43 of 143
==PAGE:52==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Figure 28: The obtained chromatogram from trial 8 with transitions and indication of the expected
retention times for THI, 2-MI and 4-MI.
5.1.4
Ion Source Parameters - Design of Experiments
Design of experiments (DOE) was used to optimize the ion source parameters for the ionisation
of THI before method validation of the imidazoles. The area under the curve (AUC) of THI
was used as the response. This response was chosen because THI had the lowest levels in real
food samples compared to the two other imidazoles (see table 1)[1]. A total of three ion source
parameters were included: Spray Voltage, Heated Probe Temperature, Cone Temperature. CID
Gas Pressure was also included in the optimisation. The four parameters were tested in the
following ranges: 2000-4000 V, 300-400 в—ҰC, 250-350в—ҰC and 1.5-2.5 mTorr, respectively. Limi-
tations were put forward in terms of which parameters could be investigated, and ranges were
chosen based on recommendations from the supplier and expert knowledge within the Research
Group for Analytical Food Chemistry at the National Food Institute.
Based on the four parameters a full-factorial design was made. A randomised design table with
2 center points and 2 replicates was generated with the software JMP [77]. The design table
and results from all 54 experiments can be seen in table 31 in appendix D. The experiments was
carried out in a spiked sample (15 ng/mL) prepared on SPE as descried in section 5.1.2. Based
on the results a half normal plot was generated to separate important factors from unimportant
ones based on their response (seen п¬Ғgure 29) [78].
Page 44 of 143
==PAGE:53==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Figure 29: Half-normal probability plot used to distinguish between important variables and unimpor-
tant ones.
The half normal plot provides strong evidence that at least п¬Ғve variables have an eп¬Җects lager that
than would be expected if they were a result of random variation. Three main variables: вҖҷCID
gas pressureвҖҷ, вҖҷSpray voltageвҖҷ and вҖҷCone temperature. Furthermore, two two-way interactions:
вҖҷSpray voltage В· Probe temperatureвҖҷ and вҖҷCID gas pressure В· Probe temperatureвҖҷ. This suggest
that these variables should be included in the model. The plot dose not indicate that the main
variable вҖҷProbe temperatureвҖҷ is important but it is incorporated in both two-way interactions
the variable should also be included in the model. A step-wise multiple regression (backward
elimination) was used to choose the signiп¬Ғcant variables. The signiп¬Ғcant (Оұ<0.05) variables
together with their respective p-values can be seen in п¬Ғgure 30. The п¬Ғnal model can be seen in
equation 14.
Figure 30: The variables chosen to be included in the model together with their respective p-values.
вҖҷОӣвҖҷ indicates that the variable is included in two-way interactions which is more signiп¬Ғcant than the main
variable it self.

The model have a R2=0.72 which means that the model can account for 72% of the variance
in the data. The p-value for the вҖҷLack of п¬Ғt testвҖҷ was 0.7751 which indicate no evidence of
lack of п¬Ғt - no signiп¬Ғcant curvature in the data is observed. The optimised response (the area
under the curve for THI) was found via a "Prediction proп¬Ғler" in JMP. In the ranges tested, the
optimal settings are as follows: 2000 V, 300 в—ҰC, 350 в—ҰC and 2.5 mTorr for Spray Voltage, Probe
Temperature, Cone Temperature and CID Gas Pressure, respectively. PaГӯga, Silva, and Delerue-
Matos [49] and Gruendling, Guilhaus, and Barner-Kowollik [50] both used DOE to optimise ion
source parameters and they both observed quadratic eп¬Җects (curvature). The authors pushed
the instrumentation setting to the limit and explored a much larger design space. If a new DOE
was to be made the instrumentation settings should be pushed further and more factors should
be included.
If more factors were to be included a full factorial design would probably not
be feasible because of the many experiments needed (198 experiments for six factors with two
center points and two replicates). A screening approach such as a Plackett-Burman design (42
experiments for six factors with two center points and two replicates) to screen for signiп¬Ғcant
factors could be a stating point. Furthermore, it can be seen that the optimised settings are at
the extremes of the ranges tested (lowest spray voltage, lowest probe temperature, highest cone
temperature and highest CID gas pressure) which could imply that there are better settings
outside of those ranges.
To conclude the DOE a comparison was made between the original and new settings for the four
parameters. 10 injections in random order of a spiked sample (15 ng/mL) was analysed with
both conп¬Ғgurations. A one-sided t-test was performed to test the hypothesis of equal mean.
The result can be seen in table 17. Since 2-MI and 4-MI will be analysed with the same method
as THI they were included in the comparison too. It can be seen that for all compounds a sig-
niп¬Ғcant larger area has been obtained with p-values well below 0.05. According to the p-value
the largest improvement was seen for 4-MI.

5.2
Extended Method Validation
5.2.1
Stability Study
The stability of HMF, 2-MI and 4-MI were investigated for a period of six weeks. THI was not
included due to lack of standard. The stability study was conducted by storing two solutions
(10 and 100 ng/mL) at four distinct conditions (at room temperature in light and dark, at 6 в—ҰC
and at вҲ’20 в—ҰC). Each week, freshly made standards for each of the two concentration level were
analysed together with the old standards stored at the aforementioned conditions (п¬Ғve injections
were made for each). Raw data can be seen in table 41-46 in appendix H.2.
The investigation was carried out without the use of internal standard since these were not
available at the time. Hence, the stability of the compounds were assessed by comparing the
responses of the old standards with the responses obtained for the freshly made standards,
and thereby calculating the stability as a recovery percent. This setup was chosen since it is
commonly known that the ion source will become dirty over time which can result in gradually
lowering of the ionisation eп¬ғciency. The comparison was made by conducting a t-test in Excel
(see p-values in table 18 and 19).
P-values below Оұ<0.05 indicates a signiп¬Ғcant diп¬Җerence
between the freshly made standard and the old standard. Signiп¬Ғcant diп¬Җerences are illustrated
by orange numbers and as it can be seen, these occur in an inconsistent manner. This can be
explained by: (1) Inconsistent handling of old standards. The ones kept at вҲ’20 в—ҰC were not
thawed properly and standards were injected before all of them reached room temperature, and
(2) Great variation of the instrument (RSD% of 10%) conп¬Ғrmed by a quality control exerted
by the equipment responsible chemist at DTU Food. In conclusion, one could that argue that
the compounds seem stable since the concentration of the standards cannot rise and fall. This
would be consistent with the п¬Ғndings of Jacobs et al. [74] which found that THI, 2-MI and 4-MI
kept at 21 в—ҰC and вҲ’20 в—ҰC over a period of four months, were stable. Despite that, the stability
of the compounds cannot be conп¬Ғrmed by this stability study.

5.2.2
Screening for HMF and 4-MI
Of the three test samples, class I contained the lowest amount of 4-MI (вҲј1.5 mg/kg) and among
the 28 manufacturer samples of class III, sample 4.2 contained lowest amount of HMF being
вҲј103 mg/kg. Test sample class I was therefore used as the matrix in the method validation for
the imidazoles and manufacturer sample 4.2 for HMF.
5.2.3
Assessment of Matrix Eп¬Җects
External calibration curves are often simple to perform in comparison to matrix matched and
standard addition calibration curves. An external calibration curve can only be utilised if either
no matrix eп¬Җects are observed or if the use of an internal standard can compensate for these
eп¬Җects. Matrix eп¬Җects are often caused by changes in the ionization eп¬ғciency of the target ana-
lyte due to the presence of other substances eluting at the same time. Matrix eп¬Җects can either
cause ion suppression or ion enhancements which will result in a decrease or an increase in the
response of the analyte, respectively [79].
To determine the extend of matrix eп¬Җects two calibration curves were constructed for each com-
pound, one acquired in Milli-Q water and one in matrix consisting of caramel colour E150c in
Milli-Q water. E150c was only free from 2-MI hence, an external calibration curve was com-
pared with a matrix matched calibration curve for 2-MI whereas external calibration curves were
compared with standard addition calibration curves for HMF, THI and 4-MI. The calibration
levels for all four types of calibration curves were as follow: 0, 1, 2.5, 5, 10, 15, 20 and 25 ng/mL.
Matrix eп¬Җects can be observed by diп¬Җerences in the slopes between the external calibration
curve and the calibration curve acquired in matrix.
Therefore, a t-test with Оұ = 0.05 was
performed to determine if the slopes were signiп¬Ғcant diп¬Җerent from one another, see table 20.
The calibration curves can be seen in the п¬Ғgures 31A, 32A, 33A and 34A for HMF, THI, 2-MI
and 4-MI, respectively. For THI a signiп¬Ғcant degree of ion suppression was observed which is
consistent with the п¬Ғndings of Goscinny et al. [4] that also found high signal suppression for
THI when determining caramel colour by-product by UHPLC-MS/MS. For 2-MI and 4-MI ion
Page 48 of 143
==PAGE:57==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
suppression was also observed but not as prominent as for THI. Goscinny et al. [4] reported
ion enhancements for 4-MI when the matrix was carbonated drinks which is not observed in
this instance. For all four compounds a signiп¬Ғcant diп¬Җerence between the slopes was observed.
To test if the use of an internal standard would be able to compensate the same experiment
was replicated but with the addition of an internal standard. The calibration curves can be
seen in the п¬Ғgures 31B, 32B, 33B and 34B for HMF, THI, 2-MI and 4-MI, respectively. The
p-values from the t-test can be seen in table 20. An internal standard was able to compensate
for the matrix eп¬Җects for all compounds. The p-value was above Оұ = 0.05 which indicates no
signiп¬Ғcant diп¬Җerence between the slopes. Based on these preliminary tests external calibration
curves with internal standard were found to be adequate for the validation of all four compounds.

5.3
Method Validation
In the following sections the result from the method validation will be presented and discussed.
Raw data from the method validation can be fond in appendices H.6, H.7, H.8 and H.9 for HMF,
THI, 2-MI and 4-MI respectively.
5.3.1
Selectivity
Selectivity between the analytes of interest was evaluated during the method development of
the separation method, recall section 5.1.1. The selectivity between the compounds of interest
and potential interference stemming from other compounds present in the matrix was evaluated
by comparing chromatograms obtained for (A) a 10 ng/mL standard, (B) a blank sample for
THI, a sample containing trace amount for 2-MI and a sample with a natural low content for
4-MI and HMF, and (C) a spiked sample (1st spiking level in the method validation for THI
and 2-MI and 2nd spiking level for 4-MI and HMF), see п¬Ғgure 35. As it can be seen, there is no
indication of interfering compounds for THI, 2-MI and HMF whereas for 4-MI slight inference
can be observed. A low peak resolution of 1.4 achieved between 4-MI and the peak to the left,
but a distinction can easily can be made between the two. Furthermore, peak broadening does
not seem to occur as the concentration increases.
Figure 35: Chromatograpic comparison of (A) standards, (B) blank sample for THI, trace amount for
2-MI and natural content for 4-MI and HMF, and (C) 1st spiking level for THI and 2-MI and 2nd spiking
level 4-MI and HMF.

5.3.2
Linearity and Sensitivity
The calibration and linearity were evaluated by making a calibration curve, residual plot and
conducting a F-test (to test for signiп¬Ғcant linearity) for each series for all four compounds. To
be able to observe if a drift occurred during the analysis four calibration curves were analysed
throughout the analysis of the spiked samples. The calibration curves were made via a weighted
regression line with the method of least squares and the weight: 1/x. A weighted regression
was chosen to give more importance/weight to the smaller concentrations and to make the
residuals more random around the zero line, see п¬Ғgure 36 for an example of a residual plot for
the calibration curve for 4-MI with and with out weights. It can be seen that the residuals
from the non-weighted regression (п¬Ғgure 36A) is more вҖҷtrumpet likeвҖҷ compared to the weighted
regression (п¬Ғgure 36B). Calibration curves for all compounds and series can be seen in appendix
H.10, in п¬Ғgures 53, 54, 55 and 56 for HMF, THI, 2-MI and 4-MI, respectively. A summary of
R2, F-value and the slope of the linear regression line can be seen in table 21. It can be seen
that the R2 is >0.99 for all compounds which indicates good linearity. Furthermore the F-value
is below Оұ=0.05 which also indicate that the calculated linearity is signiп¬Ғcant.
Figure 36: Residual plots for the calibration curve from series 1 for 4-MI (A) being the non-weighted
regression and (B) the weighted regression.

Figure 37, 38, 39 and 40 constitute examples of residual plots from series 1 for HMF, THI, 2-MI
and 4-MI, respectively which are representative for the remaining residual plots which can be
found in appendix H.10. Furthermore to ease the interpretation of the residuals in the lower
region of the calibration curves, zoomed versions of the aforementioned residual plots can be
seen in п¬Ғgure 41. The residual plots for HMF, 2-MI and 4-MI takes on a somewhat random
distribution around zero. However an increase in variation of the residuals as concentration
increases is observed. This is quite common and can sometimes be elaborated with a weighed
regression. In this case the regression is already weighed and resulted in better residual plots
(As seen in п¬Ғgure 36). For THI the increase in variation is also observed but in addition, the

indicate that the pattern may be due to a certain pipetting technique. In the low concentrations
(п¬Ғgure 41) a somewhat random distribution around zero is observed for HMF and THI with a
slight decrease as concentration increases. For 2-MI and 4-MI nearly all residuals is above zero.
Based on the residual plots, R2 and the F-test it is found that a linear model is suitable for the
method.

5.3.3
Precision and Recovery
A one way analysis of variance (ANOVA) was carried out in Excel with the Data Analysis tool
pack to calculate the repeatability (Sr) and In-House reproducibility (SR,Intra). Results from
ANOVA can be seen in appendix H.6 - H.9 and calculation examples can be seen in appendix
H.12. The Coeп¬ғcient of variation (CV%) for Sr and SR,Intra is given in table 22 for each spiking
level for all compounds. Furthermore average recoveries for each series can also be seen in table
22. According to the standard operating procedure for method validation [62] the CV% for
both the repeatability (Sr) and In-House reproducibility should be below 16% for concentra-
tions >1mg/kg. It can be seen in table 22 that this is the case for all compounds at all spike
levels. Average recoveries for each spike levels can be seen in table 22 which all lie between ac-
ceptable recoveries of 70-110%. Calculation examples of recoveries can be seen in appendix H.12.

5.3.4
LOD and LOQ
LOD and LOQ were calculated by the use of the lowest spiking level that could be validated
for all compounds. The LOD and LOQ were calculated from spiking experiments. Calculation
examples can be seen in appendix H.12. The LOD and LOQ can be seen in table 23 below.
The obtained limits are than limits reported by Jacobs et al. [74] which found a LOQ of 0.005
mg/kg for THI and 4-MI, and 0.01 mg/kg for 2-MI in soft drinks. The higher LOD achieved
for this method can be explained the diп¬Җerence in matrix. No comparable methods have been
found for the analysis of HMF, THI, 2-MI and 4-MI in pure caramel colour.

5.3.5
Ruggedness
Validation series 1 spike level 2 and the belonging calibration curve were analysed twice with a
gap of 14 days for HMF and 40 days for the imidazoles to evaluate the ruggedness of the method.
A two-sided t-test with unequal variance (tested via a F-test) was performed to compare the
concentrations. It can be seen in table 24 that there is no signiп¬Ғcant diп¬Җerences between the
results obtained for HMF and THI. For 2-MI and 4-MI there is a signiп¬Ғcant diп¬Җerence between
the п¬Ғrst analysis and the re-analysis. It can furthermore be observed that the mean concentration
is higher in the re-analysis for 2- and 4-MI. These result indicate that the internal standard used
for 2-MI and 4-MI is unstable since THI is untouched (because THI uses another IS). The
decomposition of 4-MI-d3 makes the ratio between analyte and IS larger which in turn gives a
higher concentration.

spiking level of series 1 were analysed.

The results of the quantiп¬Ғcation of HMF, THI, 2-MI and 4-MI can be seen in table 26, both
expressed in mg/kg the traditional way and in mg/kg on an equivalent colour basis. It can be
observed that the concentration of 2-MI is above the LOD estimated to be 0.085 mg/kg but just
on the border of LOQ found to be 0.169 mg/kg. During the data handling of 2-MI it was clear
that the qualifying ion peak was absent while the quantifying ion peak was visible. The S/N
ratio for the quantifying ion peak lay between 10-62 with a mean of 25. Furthermore, the reten-
tion time for the quantifying ion peak in samples had a mean value of 3.3 minutes. The same
mean retention time was observed in standards. These results indicate that 2-MI was present in
traces, in such small amounts that the qualifying ion peak was too weak to be detected and to
be used for identity conп¬Ғrmation. The results obtained for 2-MI are consistent with Goscinny et
al.[4] which found non-quantiп¬Ғable traces of 2-MI in 28 beverage samples. Since 2-MI could not
Page 57 of 143
==PAGE:66==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
be quantiп¬Ғed, the compound will not be included in further discussion. In addition, 2-MI will
not be incorporated in exposure evaluation since caramel colours are used in small amounts in
most foods and beverages which further lowers the trace amount of 2-MI. This in turn lowers the
exposure to 2-MI to a level that is not considered to pose any health risks. This is consistent with
the EFSA Scientiп¬Ғc Opinion on the re-evaluation of caramel colours (E 150 a,b,c,d) [10] in which
minimal attention is brought to 2-MI. As expected, it can be seen in table 26 that HMF was
found in quantities many times larger than that of the imidazoles. Despite this, no maximum
level (ML) has been placed for HMF, whereas the ML in EU is 10 mg/kg for THI in caramel
colour E150c, and 250 mg/kg for 4-MI in caramel colour E150c-d, both expressed on an equiv-
alent colour basis [1, 10]. As for the solids content and colour intensity, it can be observed that
certain manufacturer samples exceed the ML. Regarding THI, almost all manufacturer samples
of the type E150c exceed the ML with the exception of sample 3.4-3.6 and 4.5-4.6. For 4-MI only
three manufacturer samples exceed the ML, being sample 6.4-6.6. It is notable that the ML only
applies to the п¬Ғnal product being the last sample in each batch; hence, all п¬Ғnal products com-
ply regarding the content of 4-MI but in terms of THI, only batch 1 and 2 do not exceed the ML.

Originally, the plan was to receive a total of 18 batches. Among those, four batches of E150a,
six batches of E150c and eight batches of E150d. The aim was to investigate the inп¬Ӯuence of
diп¬Җerent process conditions. For E150a the intention was to explore how calcium carbonate and
the decrease of the heating time as a consequence, aп¬Җect the constituents levels by analysing
batches made with and without calcium carbonate. For E150c, besides evaluating the inп¬Ӯuence
of a reduced ammonia п¬Ӯow, the intention was to assess how an increase in temperature would
aп¬Җect the formation of contaminants. Lastly, for E150d, the following settings were in the scope:
original settings compared to a reduced amount of ammonium sulп¬Ғte, an increased amount of
ammonium sulп¬Ғte and an extension of the heating time.
Since few manufacturer samples were received for caramel colour E150a and E150d, and several
of the type E150d could not be expressed on an equivalent colour basis due to an absorbance
of zero, no conclusions could be drawn for these classes in relation to process conditions. There
was simply a too weak basis for interpretation. The only thing to be concluded was that both
Page 59 of 143
==PAGE:68==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
classes resulted in п¬Ғnal products with contaminant levels in compliance with previously men-
tioned regulations, which will be used in the exposure evaluation (see section 5.5). For this
reason, concentration plots for these classes are placed in appendix H.11, and will not be further
discussed in this section.
E150c Original Conditions and Reduced Ammonia Flow
To visualise how the formation of the process contaminants developed during the manufacturing
of caramel colour E150c, scatter plots have been made for each compound. In order to see if a
reduced ammonia п¬Ӯow inп¬Ӯuenced the formation of the contaminants, samples of all four batches
of E150c have been plotted together. In addition, two plots were made for each compound; п¬Ғrst,
a plot where the concentration in mg/kg (the traditional way) is plotted as a function of pro-
duction time and second, a plot where the concentrations are expressed on an equivalent colour
basis. Applicable to all plots, it should be noted that trend-lines are only for visual aid and do
not indicate measured data, and similarly, that background colours are for guidance only, since
samples were not collected at exact same times. Lastly, it is noteworthy that fewer samples were
collected during the manufacturing of the caramel colours with a reduced ammonia п¬Ӯow, which
may explain certain diп¬Җerences in the patterns observed. Almost identical time programmes
were used in the manufacture of the four batches (see appendix E for more details).
HMF in Caramel Colour E150c
Figure 42 and 43 show the plots obtained for HMF. In the п¬Ғrst plot (mg/kg the traditionally way)
it can be seen that the concentrations generally increased while ammonia was added and high
temperatures were applied, and subsequently decreased due to dilution. During the addition of
sodium hydroxide and п¬Ғltration, the trend imply that the concentration reached a point where
it became unchanged over time which could be explained by the use of lower temperatures. In
addition, it is relevant to mention that greater variation was observed for the results obtained
by the method developed for quantiп¬Ғcation of HMF compared to the to the method developed
for the imidazoles, which may also inп¬Ӯuence the pattern shown for the formation of HMF, to
some extent.
Page 60 of 143
==PAGE:69==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
Figure 42: The concentration of HMF [mg/kg] as a function of production time, batch 1-4.
Looking at п¬Ғgure 43 below, where the concentration is expressed on an equivalent colour basis, it
can be observed that the concentration dropped while ammonia was added and the temperature
was kept high.
This signify that the colour intensity of the caramel colours darkened at a
faster rate compared to formation of HMF. During dilution, addition of sodium hydroxide and
п¬Ғltration, the concentration seemed to stay the same. On an equivalent basis, this indicate that
the colour intensity of the caramel colour decreases to the same degree as the content of HMF,
when diluted. Lastly, it can be observed that the п¬Ғnal content of HMF is slightly higher for
the caramel colours with a reduced ammonia п¬Ӯow, both in mg/kg and mg/kg on an equivalent
colour basis.
Figure 43: The concentration of HMF on an equivalent colour basis [mg/kg] as a function of production
time, batch 1-4.
Page 61 of 143
==PAGE:70==

Spring 2021
THI in Caramel Colour E150c
Figure 44 and 45 show the plots obtained for THI. As for HMF, it can be observed that the
concentration of THI increased while ammonia was added and the temperature was high, and
then decreased as a consequence of dilution. Likewise, that the colour of the caramel colours
matured faster than the formation of THI. What is deviation from HMF, is that a reduced
ammonia п¬Ӯow resulted in п¬Ғnal products with a lower concentration of THI. Consequently, only
the caramel colours produced with a reduced ammonia п¬Ӯow had a concentration below the ML
for THI, being 10 mg/kg on a colour equivalent basis.
Figure 44: The concentration of THI [mg/kg] as a function of production time, batch 1-4.
Figure 45: The concentration of THI on an equivalent colour basis [mg/kg] as a function of production,
batch 1-4. *Final product exceeds the ML.
Page 62 of 143
==PAGE:71==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
4-MI in Caramel Colour E150c
Figure 46 and 47 show the concentration plots obtained for 4-MI. In п¬Ғgure 46 it can be observed
that 4-MI likewise formed rapidly during the addition of ammonia when heat was applied. What
can further be seen is that a sudden drop in concentration due to dilution was followed by an
instant increase (based on batch 1 and 2). This behavior could indicate that formation of 4-MI
to a less extent is dependent on high temperatures. As for THI, the caramel colours produced
with a reduced ammonia п¬Ӯow resulted in п¬Ғnal products with a lower content of 4-MI.
Figure 46: The concentration of 4-MI [mg/kg] as a function of production time, batch 1-4.
Figure 47: The concentration of 4-MI on an equivalent colour basis [mg/kg] as a function of production
time, batch 1-4.
Page 63 of 143
==PAGE:72==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
By comparing the batches produced according to original conditions with the batches produced
with a reduced ammonia п¬Ӯow, it was revealed that a reduction in the ammonia п¬Ӯow seemed
to lower the п¬Ғnal concentration of both imidazoles. It further suggests that a п¬Ғnal product in
compliance with the rulings for THI cannot be obtained with the use of the original process con-
ditions. Additionally, the patterns imply that too high contaminant levels cannot be solved by
dilution, when the concentration is expressed on an equivalent colour basis. This is reasonable
since addition of water will not only dilute the content of the compounds but also result in a lower
colour intensity, meaning that a larger amount of caramel colour should be used to obtain a spec-
iп¬Ғed colour. Suggestions for the manufacturer will be described in future perspectives (section 7)
From the above shown plots, it might be tempting to think that concentrations expressed on
an equivalent colour basis do not provide further basis for comparison, as the batch with the
highest content of a contaminant expressed in mg/kg (in the traditional manner) is also the
batch with the highest concentration expressed on equivalent colour basis. It is notable, that
an almost identical colour maturation is seen for the four batches of E150c. If these were to
be compared with caramel colours that follow the same formation rate of the contaminants but
with a signiп¬Ғcant diп¬Җerent colour maturation, they would appear similar when plotted normally,
but a complete diп¬Җerent pattern would be revealed when plotted on an equivalent colour basis.
Concentration on an equivalent colour basis is therefore a necessary "tool" to compare caramel
colours with diп¬Җerent colour intensities and hues in fairly manner.
Page 64 of 143
==PAGE:73==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
5.5
Exposure Evaluation
An exposure assessment has been made for two Danish population groups (adolescents and
adults), and it includes the human exposure to HMF, THI and 4-MI via caramel colours. EFSA
deп¬Ғnes adolescents as children in the age of 10-17 years, and adults as people in the age of 18-64
years [80]. No statistical information has been found for Danish people regarding the average
body weight of certain population groups. For this reason, вҖҷThe Guidance on selected default
values to be used by the EFSA Scientiп¬Ғc Committee, Scientiп¬Ғc Panels and Units in the absence
of actual measured dataвҖҷ [81] was utilised for this purpose. Based on the EFSA body weight
statistics, 70 kg should be applied as the default value for European adults whereas the mean
weight of adolescents (10-14 years) and adolescents (14-18 years) were found to be 43.4 kg and
61.3 kg, respectively. Hence, a body weight of 70 kg was chosen for adults and a body weight of
52.4 kg, being the mean of the two, was selected for adolescents (10-17 years) as shown in table 27.
Table 27: Selected Population Groups with Associated Age Ranges and Body Weights.

52.4
Contaminant Levels in Caramel Colour Classes
Own data was used to estimate the content of the three contaminants in the respective types
of caramel colour.
Since the manufacturer samples were taken out at diп¬Җerent steps in the
manufacturing process, they do not represent real market samples. The most accurate estimates
were therefore believed to be obtained by only considering the manufacturer samples that best
imitate п¬Ғnal products, being the last sample in each batch. A total of four batches were received
for caramel colour E150c. Therefore, the amount of HMF, THI and 4-MI in E150c were based
on the manufacturer sample containing the highest amount without exceeding the maximum
levels (ML) and without falling outside the speciп¬Ғcations in terms of solids content and colour
intensity.
The ML in EU is 10 mg/kg for THI and 250 mg/kg for 4-MI, both on a colour
equivalent basis [10]. For class I, the amount of HMF was based on manufacturer sample 5.4.
For class III the amounts of HMF and THI were based on manufacturer sample 4.6 and the
amount of 4-MI on manufacturer sample 2.8. Lastly, for class IV the amounts of HMF and
4-MI were based on manufacturer sample 6.7. The received manufacturer samples only covered
caramel colour class I, III and IV, hence the amounts of HMF and 4-MI (THI not expected) in
class II were based on the caramel colour safety update by Vollmuth [1]. In this, no data was
available for 4-MI and a range of 3300-33700 mg/kg was given for HMF for caramel colour class
II. The level of 4-MI was therefore set as zero and a concentration of 18500 mg/kg was chosen for
HMF since own manufacturer samples contained amounts in both the lower and upper region of
the constituent ranges given for the other classes of caramel colour. Table 28 below summarise
the selected concentrations.

Caramel Colour Levels in Foodstuп¬Җs and Beverages
The maximum permitted level of caramel colour (E150a-d) in all food and beverage categories
in which caramel colours are utilised, are set as вҖҷquantum satisвҖҷ according to the commission
regulation (EU) No 1129/2011 of 11 November 2011 amending Annex II to Regulation (EC) No
1333/2008 [9]. Therefore, reported usage levels were applied to estimate the content of caramel
colour in relevant food and beverage items. EFSA made use of maximum reported use levels
when re-evaluating caramel colours [10]. In the re-evaluation, it was assessed if the daily total
intake of caramel colour exceeds the group ADI of 300 mg/day, and based on the immunotoxicity
of THI an individual ADI of 100 mg/day was established for caramel colour class III.
The scope of this exposure assessment was to evaluate the human exposure to the compounds of
interest via caramel colours rather than the human exposure to caramel colour itself. Since the
amount of caramel colour needed to obtain a speciп¬Ғed colour is highly dependent on the colour
intensity, it is assumed that use of uncommonly high doses has been due to low colour inten-
sities. Thus, the anticipated levels of caramel colour in the included foodstuп¬Җs and beverages
were based on highest typical use levels rather than maximum reported use levels listed in the
EFSA scientiп¬Ғc opinion [10] and provided by the European Technical Committee on Caramels
(EUTECA)[82], the Confederation of the Food and Drink Industry of the EU, CIAA[83] and
Tennant, David R [84]. For food and beverage categories where more than a single value was
given, the larger of them was chosen (exact values can be seen in table 86 and 87 in appendix
H.13 for foodstuп¬Җs and beverages, respectively). Lastly, it is notable that the content of caramel
colour used in beverages is given in amount per liter and the consumption data discussed in the
next paragraph is given in grams. Thus, for simplicity it was assumed that 1 L equals 1 kg.
Daily Intake of Foodstuп¬Җs, Beverages and Contaminants
Consumption data was gathered from the EFSA Comprehensive European Food Consumption
Database [85], and data was extracted for "All subjects" and not "Consumers only" to get a more
realistic estimate of the average intake of the great variety of food and beverage in which caramel
colours are used. To only include consumption of foods and beverages containing caramel colour,
data was collected from the fourth and п¬Ғfth Food consumption statistics level. A total of 16
food and 7 beverage categories were found to fall within the categories listed in the EFSA re-
evaluation of caramel colours [10], and for each of the two population groups, the mean and 95th
percentile intakes were chosen. The 95th percentile was used rather than the 97.5th percentile
since data to little data was available to ensure statistical robust 97.5th percentile. Categories
without Danish consumption data were compensated by the use of data from the UK popula-
tion. Table 88 in appendix H.13 summarises the anticipated intakes of the selected food and
Page 66 of 143
==PAGE:75==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
beverage items. The daily intake of caramel colour class I-IV was estimated by combining the
consumption data and the highest typical use levels for each class. For categories where more
than one class of caramel colour can be applied, an equal distribution was chosen due to lack
of information and simplicity. The mean and 95th percentile intake for adults can be seen table
89 and 90, respectively, and likewise the mean and 95th percentile intake for adolescents can be
seen table 91 and 92, respectively (all in appendix H.13).
The total exposure in adults and adolescents was found to be approximately half of what EFSA
found [10]. These results are expected since EFSA made use of maximum reported use levels
of caramel colours which is much higher than reported typical use levels used in the current
exposure evaluation.

Evaluation of the Dietary Exposure
The total amount of caramel colors consumed in a day and the anticipated amount of contami-
nants present in each of class (recall table 28) were used to estimate the daily dietary exposure
to HMF, THI and 4-MI via caramel colours. The daily exposure of each compound and the
associated margin of exposure can be seen in table 30. Based on the toxicity of the compounds
discussed in section 3.1, the NOAEL values used in this assessment were 80 mg/kg bw/day for
HMF (acute and sub-acute toxicity), 250 Вөg/kg bw/day for THI (immunosuppressive activity)
and 80 mg/kg bw/day for 4-MI (carcinogenic eп¬Җects).

A MOE >100 for non-carcinogenic eп¬Җects and a MOE >10000 for carcinogenic eп¬Җects gener-
ally imply low health risks, but known uncertainties and limitations of the exposure assessment
should be considered as they have a direct impact on the obtained MOE values. Uncertainties
and limitations will be discussed in next subsection.
As shown in table 30, the MOE for HMF is below the recommended threshold of 100 for both
95th percentiles, which are consistent with literature. According to Monakhova Yulia B [86],
Page 67 of 143
==PAGE:76==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
which looked at the human exposure to HMF via alcoholic beverages in Europe, a MOE of 465
was obtained in the worst case scenario. Arribas-Lorenzo and Morales [87] studied the exposure
to HMF from coп¬Җee in Spain and reported MOEвҖҷs in the range of 576-1051. Taking into account
that the current exposure evaluation include all food and beverages descried above a lower MOE
is to be expected. These results indicate that the exposure to HMF via caramel colours could
be of public health concern.
The exposure to THI was found to be in the range of 0.067-0.538 Вөg/kg bw/day. This is con-
sistent with other studies done on the area. Liang et al. [88] evaluated the human exposure to
THI in the Chinese population via intake of beverages in which caramel colour E150c has been
added. The intake was found to be in the range of 0.1вҖ“0.3 Вөg/kg bw/day. Liang et al. [88]
found exposures to THI from E150c in beverages in China in the range 0.1вҖ“0.3 Вөg/kg bw/day
and concluded that the exposure to THI through E150c was of low public health concern. The
MOE for THI was below the threshold of 100 which also indicate that the exposure to THI
through E150c is of low public health concern for the Danish population.
The exposure to 4-MI was found to be in the range of 0.0035-0.0130 mg/kg bw/day which is
also consistent with other п¬Ғndings. Liang et al. [88] found exposures to 4-MI from E150c,d in
China to be in the range of 0.012-0.0271 mg/kg bw/day and a MOE of 11000 in the worst case
scenario regarding consumption. The inclusion of a wider variety of foods and beverages result
in a slightly lower MOE of 10000 for both 95th percentiles which is below the threshold. These
results indicate that the exposure to 4-MI from caramel colours might be of public health concern.
Uncertainty Considerations and Recapitulation
вҖў As an alternative to NOAEL a BMD10 could be used as this make better use of the data
obtained from animal studies. Furthermore, BMDL10 provides higher conп¬Ғdence in the
estimate because it takes the statistical limitations of the data into account. Data for this
purpose was not found.
вҖў Many studies have been conducted on the carcinogenic eп¬Җects of HMF but uncertainties
still remains if these eп¬Җects exist at all and the eп¬Җects are through a threshold mechanism.
If these eп¬Җects turns out to be true a higher safety factor of 10000 should be used. Fur-
thermore, studies on other endpoints (e.g. reproductive toxicity) is needed as these may
have a lower NOAEL than acute and sub acute eп¬Җects.
вҖў Calculations were done under the assumption that all the included food and beverage
items contained caramel colors, which is not the case. E.g. not all soft drinks contains
caramel colours. Thus, in regards to consumption of caramel colours an overestimation
has been made.
вҖў The concentration of HMF, THI and 4-MI used in these calculations was based on own
data from a single supplier. It is known that the concentration of the contaminants in
caramel colours varies a lot which brings more uncertainty to the exposure assessment.
Page 68 of 143
==PAGE:77==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
вҖў 2-MI was not included in the exposure evaluation because only trace amounts were found
in samples. Instead of excluding the compound it could be considered using 1/2В·LOD or
LOD as the concentration.
вҖў Consumption data from the Danish population is scarce. For some food and beverage cat-
egories data from the UK population was used. It is unknown how the eating habits diп¬Җer
between Denmark and the UK. One could consider using data from other Scandinavian
countries as these might represent the eating habits of the Danish population better.

==PAGE:78==

Spring 2021
6
Conclusion
A single method was sought for the simultaneous quantiп¬Ғcation of HMF, THI, 2-MI and 4-MI
in caramel colours. Since diп¬Җerent sample preparation procedures were needed and HMF was
found in concentrations many times higher than that of the imidazoles, two distinct UHPLC-
MS/MS methods were developed and validated. A combined method was simply not achievable
since the strong cation exchange SPE procedure necessary for the analysis of the imidazoles
was not compatible with HMF (could not be retained in the cartridge). Prior to the method
validation, the extend of matrix eп¬Җects were investigated. Ion suppression was observed for all
four compounds but the use of internal standards could compensate for these eп¬Җects. Thus,
external calibrations were found suitable for the methods.
An optimised separation method for the imidazoles resulted in a longer retention time for THI
(from 1.66 to 1.99 min) and better peak resolutions (from 1.87 to 2.38 between THI and 2-
MI; and from 1.18 to 2.08 between 2-MI and 4-MI) compered to literature. This was obtained
by adjusting the column oven temperature, п¬Ӯow rate and gradient programme. In addition,
optimisation of the sample preparation procedure resulted in better peak shapes, and optimi-
sation of the ion source parameters with respect to THI resulted in a signiп¬Ғcant enhancement
of the response for all three imidazoles. In conclusion, the method developed for the imidazoles
demonstrated great recoveries of 98.4-107.5%, 84.2-103.8% and 87.1-110.9% across the ranges of
1.25-10, 1.25-140 and 1.54-140 mg/kg for THI, 2-MI and 4-MI, respectively. Furthermore, the
LOD and LOQ were estimated to be 0.055 and 0.110 mg/kg for THI, 0.085 and 0.169 mg/kg for
2-MI, and 0.974 and 1.948 mg/kg for 4-MI, respectively. Great precision was achieved for all an-
alytes with CV%< 2.7 for repeatability and CV%< 3.6 for In-House reproduciability. For HMF,
the lowest spiking level could not validated due to a great variation CV% = 53.5 (repeatability)
and CV% = 60.2 (In-House reproduciability) which was possibly due to homogeneity issues.
For the levels that could be validated a recovery of 80.0-110.7% was obtained over the range of
300-35000 mg/kg, 300 mg/kg being the LOQ. A CV%< 3.3 for repeatability and CV%< 12.0
for In-House reproduciability were achieved for HMF. Both methods demonstrated R2 >0.998
in the calibration ranges being 1-300 ng/mL for the imidazoles and 1-200 mg/kg for HMF.
Lastly, the newly developed methods have been applied successfully to evaluate the impact of
a lowered ammonia п¬Ӯow in the production of caramel colour E150c. The quantiп¬Ғcation of the
constituents revealed that a lower ammonia п¬Ӯow seemed to lower the п¬Ғnal content of THI and
4-MI (both expressed traditionally and on an equivalent colour basis), and that use of original
conditions may result in п¬Ғnal products exceeding the ML of THI being 10 mg/kg on equivalent
colour basis. In conclusion, an exposure assessment was conducted for two Danish population
groups (adults and adolescents) to evaluate if the intake of HMF, THI and 4-MI stemming via
caramel colours pose a public health concern. For certain scenarios, it was found that the MOE
of HMF and 4-MI was below the threshold. This implies that HMF and 4-MI could be a health
risk for the Danish population.
Page 70 of 143
==PAGE:79==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
7
Future Perspectives
Tailing of 2-MI and 4-MI
Despite a good separation method was obtained in terms of resolution and retention times, tailing
was observed for 2-MI and 4-MI. Tailing can result in inconsistent integration of peaks because
no clear peak boundary is present. It would improve the method if this could be resolved. The
following factors could be investigated:
вҖў If more than one retention mechanism is causing the tailing. A highly deactivated column
could be investigated to silanol activity.
вҖў A guard column was used during this project which was never changed.
If the guard
column has failed this might contribute to the problem. This is not likely, since tailing of
2-MI and 4-MI have been reported in literature [4, 16, 13].
Method validation and sample preparation
In general the method validation was successfully but improvements for the residual plots might
be possible. The weight used for the regression was 1/x to give more importance to the smaller
concentrations. Small tendencies was observed in which the residuals dropped over time. Other
weight could investigated to see of these tendencies could be removed.
A great variation was seen for HMF in the lowest spike level of the validation being the natural
content of the sample containing the lowest amount of HMF. Less variation may be achieved
by striving for a better homogenisation. This could possible be obtained by taking out a larger
fraction of the sample and pre-dilute it prior to the sample preparation procedure in question.
In addition, a lower spiking level than that of 300 mg/kg could be investigated.
Manufacturing conditions and supplier recommendations
It could be interesting to examine to a greater extent the manufacturing of caramel colours to
reveal which parameters that haven the greatest inп¬Ӯuence on the formation of the substances.
It could be interesting to develop a mathematical that could describe the relationship between
process conditions and the formation of the substances. This could possibly be done by the
approach of DOE. Each batch of caramel colour might be expensive to produce and it is therefore
important to obtain as much information about the process in as few experiments as possible - it
is here DOE makes a diп¬Җerence. DOE makes it possible to identify interactions between variables
such as time and temperature without carrying out all combinations of setting - which might be
numerous. The mathematical method could be used to predict concentration of by-products, п¬Ғnd
optimised combination of process conditions that could reduce cost (e.g. by reducing reactants
needed, lowering temperatures or reduce production time) and simultaneously make sure that
the product comply with regulation.

A
Appendix: Chemical Risk Assesment (APV)
Master Thesis: Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
вҖ“ Method Development, Validation and Exposure Evaluation.
Project duration: 01-03-2021 to 05-09-2021
Issued by: Nadia KlГјver Tronier Mikkelsen and Mads Lysgaard Hansen
METHOD
Category 1 - HMF
For both procedures, 100 mg of caramel colour was transferred to a 100 mL volumetric п¬Ӯask
and п¬Ғlled to volume with Milli-Q water after which it was thoroughly mixed.
Following
procedure 1, 500 ВөL of the sample mix and 0.1 mL of IS (1000 ng/mL) were transferred to a
100 mL volumetric п¬Ӯask and п¬Ғlled to volume with Milli-Q water. Following procedure 2, 500
ВөL of the sample mix and 1 mL of IS (100 ng/mL) were transferred to a 10 mL volumetric
п¬Ӯask and likewise п¬Ғlled to volume. After thorough mixing 250 ВөL of the dilution was then
п¬Ғltered through a Mini-UniPrepTM syringe less п¬Ғlter with a pore size of 0.2 Вөm.
Category 2 - Imidazoles
Prior to the sample dilution, 1250 ВөL of the internal standards 13C6-THI (for THI) and 4-
MI-d3 (for 2-MI and 4-MI), both with a concentration of 1000 ng/mL were added to 100
mg sample in a weighing boat and gently mixed with a glass spatula. The isotope labelled
compounds were added to compensate for potential matrix eп¬Җects and gently mixed with a
glass spatula again. The mix was then put to rest for 30 minutes after which it was transferred
to a 100 mL volumetric п¬Ӯasks and brought to volume with Milli-Q water. A volume of 500
ВөL of the diluted sample (1000 mg/L) and 20 ВөL 0.1 M HCL were added to a volumetric п¬Ӯask
and brought to 3 mL with Milli-Q water. A SPE column of the type StrataTM-X-C 33 Вөm
Polymeric Strong Cation was conditioned with 2 mL of methanol followed by activation with
2 mL 0.1 M HCl and then loading with the 3 mL sample mix. To ensure ionic exchange, the
prepared sample was slowly passed through the cartridge after which it was washed with 2
mL of methanol. The elution was carried out with 4 mL of a 1.5% ammonium hydroxide in
methanol. The elute was evaporated to dryness under a gentle stream of Nitrogen (2 L/min)
at 40 в—ҰC. The dry extract was dissolved in 250 ВөL of Milli-Q water. The extract was п¬Ғltered
through a 0.2 Вөm Mini-UniPrepTM syringe less п¬Ғlter vial and analysed.

SIGNIFICANT HAZARDS FROM CHEMICALS
5-(Hydroxymethyl)furfural (CAS no.: 67-47-0) [89]
вҖў H315: Causes skin irritation
вҖў H319: Causes serious eye irritation
вҖў H335: May cause respiratory irritation
вҖў H412: Harmful to aquatic life with long lasting eп¬Җects
вҖў P261: Avoid breathing vapour, aerosol, or dust
вҖў P273: Avoid release to the environment
вҖў P280: Wear protective gloves/eye protection
вҖў P305+351+338: IF IN EYES: Rinse cautiously with water for several minutes. Remove
contact lenses, if present and easy to do. Continue rinsing
вҖў "Caution - this substance has not yet been fully researched"
5-Hydroxymethyl-2-furaldehyde-13C6 (CAS no.: 1219193-98-2) [90]
вҖў H315: Causes skin irritation
вҖў H319: Causes serious eye irritation
вҖў H335: May cause respiratory irritation
вҖў H402: Harmful to aquatic life.
вҖў P261: Avoid breathing dust/fume/gas/mist/vapours/spray.
вҖў P264: Wash hands thoroughly after handling.
вҖў P273: Avoid release to the environment.
вҖў P280: Wear protective gloves/protective clothing/eye protection/face protection.
вҖў P305/P351/P338: IF IN EYES: Rinse cautiously with water for several minutes. Re-
move contact lenses, if present and easy to do. Continue rinsing.
2-Methylimidazole (CAS no.: 693-98-1)[89]
вҖў H302: Harmful if swallowed
вҖў H314: Causes severe skin burns and eye damage
вҖў H351: Suspected of causing cancer
вҖў H360Df: May damage the unborn child. Suspected of damaging fertility
вҖў P201: Obtain special instructions before use

SIGNIFICANT HAZARDS OF THE WORK PROCESS
Handling of methanol, formic acid and ammonia constituted the main hazards of the work
process
Page 86 of 143
==PAGE:95==
Study of Process Contaminants in Caramel Colours by LC-MS/MS
Spring 2021
RISK OF HEALTH IMPACT
The following consideration has been made regarding the laboratory work:
вҖў When mixing water and acid, it is of great importance that the acid is added to the
water and not the other way around. A vigorous exothermic reaction takes place when
water and acid are mixed which releases heat, sometimes to a point where it causes the
liquid to boil. Thus, when water is added to acid, you initially form a concentration
solution that may boil extremely violently.
SUBSTITUTIONS CONSIDERATIONS
Chlorinated agent was needed in the method (SALLE). The method was changed to avoid
these agents because of health risks.

WHAT TO DO IN THE CASE OF ACCIDENT AND WASTE?
In the case of spillage of formic acid, methanol and 25% ammonium solution:
вҖў If spillage occurs one should wear respiratory protection and ensure adequate ventilation
to avoid breathing vapours, mist or gas. All sources of ignition should be removed and
personnel should be evacuated to a safe areas.
Vapours can accumulating to form
explosive concentrations.
вҖў Further leakages or spillage should be stopped but only if it safe to do so. The product
must not enter the drain and discharge into the environment must be avoided.
вҖў Limit the spillage and collect with an electrically protected vacuum cleaner or by wet-
brushing and place in appropriate container for disposal.
вҖў Only for ammonium 25%: Neutralise with Hydrochloric acid and Wipe with a mop.
WASTE
The following compounds belong to waste group H:
вҖў 5-(Hydroxymethyl)furfural (CAS no.: 67-47-0)
вҖў 2-Methylimidazole (CAS no.: 693-98-1)
вҖў 4(5)-Methylimidazole (CAS no.: 822-36-6)
вҖў 4-Methylimidazole-d3 (CAS no.: 1219805-95-4):
вҖў 2-Acetyl-4(5)-tetrahydroxybutylimidazol (CAS no.: 94944-70-4)
вҖў Ammonia solution 25% (CAS no.: 1336-21-6)
вҖў Uracil (CAS no: 66-22-8)
вҖў HCl 1M (CAS no:7647-01-0)
вҖў Formic acid 98-100% (CAS no: 64-18-6)
The following compounds belong to waste group C:
вҖў Methanol (CAS no: 67-56-1)
вҖў Ethanol (CAS no: 64-17-5)
PREGNANT AND BREASTFEEDING
Pregnant and breastfeeding woman should not work with the chemicals and methods de-
scribed.
SUGGESTIONS FOR IMPROVING THE SECURITY:
An improvement could be to only allow closed footwear in the laboratory areas. This could
reduce the risk of injury caused by dropping of chemicals, glassware and similar.

B
Appendix: Ion Tuning
A multiple reaction monitoring (MRM) method was developed for each compound by the use
of the software вҖҷBruker MS WorkstationвҖҷ. Ion tuning was done by direct infusion of standard
solutions with a concentration of 100 ppb in 50% (V/V) methanol in Milli-Q water. Standard
solutions of higher concentrations were used if an MRM method was not obtained in the п¬Ғrst
go. A syringe pump was used to infuse the solution into the Electron spray ionisation (ESI)
source and a п¬Ӯow rate of 5 ВөL/min was chosen. The source parameters were set as follows:
вҖў Spray voltage: 4000V for positive and 3500V for negative.
вҖў Cone gas: 20 L/Hr
вҖў Cone temperature: 350 в—ҰC
вҖў Probe gas: 0 L/Hr
вҖў Probe temperature: 50 в—ҰC
вҖў Nebulizer gas п¬Ӯow: 10 L/Hr
вҖў Exhaust gas: Oп¬Җ

F
Appendix: Extraction and Puriп¬Ғcation Trials
Two diп¬Җerent extraction approaches were tested in diп¬Җerent settings, namely SALLE and SPE.
A total of eight trials were carried out for SALLE from which trial one and two were performed
to visual assess if a phase separation took place with the use of methanol instead of ethanol.
Moreover, to п¬Ғnd the suп¬ғcient amount of salt (Di-Potassium hydrogen phosphate, K2HPO4)
the amount was varied from 0.5-3 g while the volumes of methanol and Milli-Q water/ sample
dilution were kept constant as shown in table 38. For a single trial Ammonia was added.

Method Validation: Precision
The Precision of the methods was calculated by using an one-sided variance analysis in Excel.
The following calculation examples are based on the Single Factor ANOVA performed for 4-MI
spiking level 3 (see table 84 and 85).

Conversion from ng/mL to mg/kg
Since diп¬Җerent sample preparation procedures were developed for the analysis of the compounds,
three diп¬Җerent equations were utilised to convert the concentrations from the unit given in the
calibration curves (ng/mL) to actual amounts in the samples (mg/kg). For category 1 (HMF)
two dilution procedures were established; Equation 24 follows the dilution procedure 1 being 5
mg/L of caramel colour in Milli-Q, and equation 25 follows the dilution procedure 2, being 50
mg/L caramel colour in Milli-Q water. For category two consisting of the imidazoles equation
26 was used for the conversion.
Concentration[mg/kg] =
(Xng/mL В· 100mL) В· 200
0.1 g В· 106ng/mg

В· 10000 В· 0.1 g/kg
(24)
Concentration[mg/kg] =
(Xng/mL В· 10mL) В· 200
0.1 g В· 106ng/mg

В· 10000 В· 0.1 g/kg
(25)
Equation 24 and 25 have the following explanation:

вҖў X ng/mL = The concentration of the sample in relation to the calibration curve.
вҖў 100 mL or 10 mL = The п¬Ғnal volume.
вҖў 200 = Correspond to an initial 200 folds dilution.
вҖў 0.1 g = The amount of sample used.
вҖў 106 ng/mg = Conversion from ng to mg.
вҖў 10000 В· 0.1 g/kg = Conversion from 0.1 g to kg.
Concentration[mg/kg] =
(Xng/mL В· 0.25 mL) В· 200
0.1 g В· 106ng/mg

В· 10000 В· 0.1 g/kg
(26)
Equation 26 has the following explanation:
вҖў X ng/mL = The concentration of the sample in relation to the calibration curve.
вҖў 0.25 mL = The п¬Ғnal volume subsequent to the SPE procedure.
вҖў 200 = A 200 folds dilution carried out step-wise in the extraction procedure.
вҖў 0.1 g = The amount of sample used.
вҖў 106 ng/mg = Conversion from ng to mg.
вҖў 10000 В· 0.1 g/kg = Conversion from 0.1 g to kg.
